2.10     The IDA Credit would be made available to the Kingdom of Nepal on standard terms.
The Kingdom of Nepal would pass on its share of the total project cost as a grant to the Board
based upon work programs agreed between the Board and the Kingdom of Nepal. The project
would provide grant funding for Board establishment and operations (US$4.98 million); selection
and construction of water supply and sanitation schemes (US$15.23 million); and institutional
development and studies (US$1.04 million).
III. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
Status of Project Preparation
3.01     The project has been prepared in four stages. First, a UNDP-funded team of local and
expatriate consultants identified the project concept. Second, following the Kingdom of Nepal's
decision to proceed with more detailed preparation, a team (with some of the same members as
the first) prepared a detailed project proposal, with funding from a Japanese Grant Facility. Third,
a second Japanese Grant has, from March 1993, financed field testing of a trial Rural Water
Supply and Sanitation Fund Development Board (commonly known by its Nepali acronym
Jakpas) to contract with private entities, including NGOs, in order to assist communities to plan,
implement, operate, and maintain rural water supply and sanitation schemes in a participatory
manner. Fourth, a third Japanese Grant has expanded the field testing until mid-1996. The field
testing has permitted us to finalize: (a) the draft Board Formation Order; (b) draft rules of the
Board Formation Order; (c) a draft Board implementation manual, including an operations
manual, a training manual, a monitoring and evaluation manual, a technical manual, a Board
accounting manual and an SO accounting manual, and a standard unit price system; (d) a 36-
month scheme cycle detailing all critical steps; (e) a management information system; and (f) unit
costs for all project components. The pilot project has also identified a pipeline of 108 schemes
for development during the first year of Board operation. Draft development phase contracts for
these schemes are ready for Board signing.
Organization and Manazement
3.02     The Board was created on March 14, 1996 through cabinet approval of a Formation
Order (Annex 4) under the Development Board Act (Annex 5). The MHPP will be the line



- 16 -
ministry for the Board. The Board's main objective is to promote sustainable, reliable, and cost-
effective demand-led rural water supply and sanitation services with emphasis on community
based approaches.
3.03      The Board will be managed by a Board of seven members, at least one will be a
woman. Four members will represent the public sector, with representatives from the National
Planning Commission, the Ministry of Finance, the Ministry of Local Development, and the
Ministry of Housing and Physical Planning, and three members will come from the private sector
(including two members from NGOs). Board members will be selected according to qualification
and criteria defined in the Board Formation Order. The Formation Order indicates that for the
first two years of Board operation the Secretary of the MNHPP would be the Chairperson of the
Board. After these first two years the Chairperson of the Board will be elected by Board
members. The Formation Order stipulates that the Board may frame its own rules, provided that
it consults the Ministry of Housing and Physical Planning on rules related to staff recruitment
procedures, salary, allowances, and other benefits. During negotiations, assurances were obtained
that the Kingdom of Nepal would take all action necessary on its part to enable the Board to
formulate and implement principles and procedures for the recruitment, employment and
compensation of its staff
3.04      Board staff will be independent of the Kingdom of Nepal's civil service rules and
regulations and will be recruited only on contractual terms through open competition and
according to qualifications and criteria defined in the rules. The rules define staffing, terms and
conditions of employment of Board staff (including salary and allowances), financial and
procurement regulations of the Board, and support organization and scheme eligibility criteria.
All staff will have well-defined responsibilities. Staff performance would be monitored regularly
and rewarded through an appropriate incentive system. The draft terms of reference for senior
staff have been prepared and would be attached to the draft rules. Draft rules were reviewed and
discussed during credit negotiations. Board adoption of rules satisfactory to the Bank would be
a condition for disbursement for schemes.
3.05      All Board operating procedures are further detailed and presented in the Board
implementation manual, part of the Board rules. The Board implementation manual includes an
operations manual, a training manual, a monitoring and evaluation manual, a technical manual, an
SO accounting manual and a Board accounting manual, and a standard unit price system. Board
adoption of an implementation manual satisfactory to the Bank would be a condition for
disbursement for schemes.
3.06      The Board will be responsible for Board policy and for monitoring its implementation.
All necessary powers to approve the budgets, programs, and plans, and to approve the selection
of support organizations and schemes would be conferred on the Board to allow it to work in a
flexible and fully accountable manner. The Board will be responsible for project implementation.
The Board will subcontract with support organizations and communities that meet eligibility
criteria (defined in the rules (Annex 6) of the Board Formation Order) to assist beneficiary
communities to plan and implement water supply and sanitation schemes that meet established
eligibility criteria. The Board will contract service agencies for activities such as project



- 17 -
promotion, training, appraisal, monitoring and evaluation, research and development, and auditing
of Board accounts. It would also be responsible for administering all project-funded studies.
3.07     Secretariat.  Board policies will be implemented by its Secretariat, consisting of 30
staff They include six managers (the Executive Director, the Deputy Executive Director, and
four unit chiefs), four officers, six portfolio managers (as the number of schemes increases,
portfolio managers would increase to a total of fifteen), and fourteen support staff.  The
organizational structure of the Board is presented in Annex 18. The Secretariat would be headed
by the Executive Director (ED), who would be assisted by a Deputy Executive Director (DED).
The ED was recruited and appointed by the Board according to qualifications and criteria in the
Board Formation Order and its rules.
3.08     The ED, with Board approval, will recruit and appoint all professional staff according
to criteria established in the rules. In addition, a long-term technical advisor (for terms of
reference see Annex 17) with expertise and experience in institutional development, management,
and technical aspects of the Board's work will be recruited for a two-year period, and several
months of short-term technical assistance will be made available to assist the ED in managing the
Board's work program. During negotiations assurances were obtained that a qualified technical
advisor would be recruited by the Board before January 1, 1997.
3.09     The Secretariat will be organized into four units: the Administration Unit (including a
financial analyst and an accounts officer), the Program Development Unit (human resources
development specialists), the Technical Appraisal Unit (sanitary engineers) and an
Operations/Monitoring and Evaluation Unit (including a monitoring and evaluation specialist
assisted by a statistician or data specialist). Detailed qualifications are defined in the rules. The
Board will subcontract service agencies as needed to assist with portfolio management. At least
two of the six managers will be women to ensure that gender issues are addressed, and concerted
efforts will be made to involve beneficiary women in rural water supply and sanitation activities
for scheme sustainability.
3.10     The Board Formation Order provides the ED with full autonomy to manage its
program and personnel. The ED would be the ex officio secretary of the Board and is the link
between policy-making and execution. The ED will chair a technical appraisal committee which
will screen, select, and send for Board review and approval, support organization and scheme
proposals. The technical appraisal committee will consist of the ED, the DED, the technical
advisor, and four unit chiefs. In order to ensure proper coordination of activities at the local level,
the Board will require support organizations to inform concerned local authorities of scheme
selection. The ED is responsible for preparing annual programs and budgets to be reviewed and
approved by the Board. Once approved by the Board, these budgets will be reviewed and
approved by the National Planning Commission. The Ministry of Finance will subsequently
release funds to the Board on a grant basis. The ED will be accountable to the Board for overall
implementation performance of the Board. During negotiations, agreement was obtained that the
Kingdom of Nepal will make the proceeds of the Credit available to the Board, under a grant
agreement to be entered into between the Kingdom of Nepal and the Board, under terms and



- 18 -
conditions satisfactory to the Bank, including programs requesting the Board to utilize the
proceeds of the Credit to provide grants to develop and implement rural water supply and
sanitation schemes in accordance with the provisions of the Formation Order, the rules and the
implementation manual.
Implementation Stratezv
3.11     Project implementation period. The proposed project will give the Kingdom of
Nepal a strategy to implement its policy of decentralized service delivery with greater private
sector involvement. For this reason the proposed project has a strong emphasis on institutional
development and testing an alternative service delivery approach. It does not intend to achieve
full coverage of water and sanitation throughout Nepal, but instead covers a five-year period of
sectoral investments (1996 - 2001). The project completion date is September 30, 2001. Scheme
selection will follow a demand-driven approach whereby communities would need to confirm
need, economic viability, technical feasibility, sustainability, and willingness to pay before they are
selected. The project will finance improvements in water supply and sanitation services for 900
communities in four batches under the implementation phase of the scheme cycle. The first batch
will cover 93 communities, the second batch 185, the third batch 260 and the fourth batch 362.
Given the estimated community dropout rate of about 13%, the project will need to prepare a
total of 1035 communities divided into four batches of 107, 213, 299 and 416, respectively under
the development phase of the scheme cycle. It is anticipated that the absorptive capacity of
support organizations will permit us to add up to 100 new communities to each batch. These
schemes would serve a design population of about 0.55 million, of whom about 0.39 million live
in the hills and about 0.16 million in the Terai. A mid-term review would be carried out during
the third year of project implementation to assess performance and redesign the project as
necessary. Assuming the concept and design adopted for the project are successful, funding has
been provided for a follow-up project to be prepared towards the end of the project
implementation period. The follow-up project, if it materializes, will probably seek to apply the
lessons learned from the proposed project, provide a framework to consolidate the institution (the
Board) created under it, and substantially increase rural water supply and sanitation service
coverage. A project implementation schedule is shown in Annex 7.
3.12     To  increase ownership  and  sustainability, communities, assisted  by  support
organizations, will plan, design, implement, operate, and maintain their schemes. The project will
thus be implemented by beneficiary communities, represented by Water User Committees (WUCs)
with support from  support organizations.   The community-based approach to  scheme
identification, design, construction, operation, and maintenance moves the emphasis from physical
infrastructure to community development activities in order to increase beneficiary participation in
decision-making, in implementing its decisions, and in sharing its benefits.
3.13     Scheme cycle.  The sequence of actions required from scheme identification to
completion has been defined within the scheme cycle (Annex 19). The scheme cycle takes into
account seasons, harvests, and holidays, and their influence on site accessibility, water source
yield measurement, availability of labor, and conditions and timing for construction work, as well



- 19 -
as the time needed to process proposals. The scheme cycle would span 36 calendar months and
includes three main phases (pre-development, development and implementation). The detailed
scheme cycle (tested and refined under the Jakpas pilot project) identifies 190 steps and their
respective duration.  The scheme cycle and its review process are described in the Board
operations manual.  The three main phases of the scheme cycle, and their objectives and
implementation details are described below.
3.14      The pre-development phase of the scheme cycle lasts about 13 months and begins in
October of any given year. Its main objective is to identify support organizations (SOs) and
schemes that meet eligibility criteria to enter into a development phase contract. The pre-
development phase includes the four following activities:
*  Pre-qualification of SOs: The Board Secretariat will identify SOs through a media
campaign which requests them  to forward their expression of  interest.  Once
applications are received the Board Secretariat would assess SO capacity, based upon
a review of their applications and field visits to assess their performance in previous
activities. After capacity is assessed the Board Secretariat would hold a technical
appraisal committee meeting to review whether all SO eligibility criteria (para. 3.24)
have been met before sending its recommendations to the Board for final SO pre-
qualification. Pre-qualification requires about four months and is completed by end-
January of the first year. At this stage the Board Secretariat assigns one of its staff to
follow all future contacts between the Board and the pre-qualified SO and its portfolio
of schemes. This person is called a portfolio manager.
*  Pre-feasibility study preparation and assessment. Pre-qualified SOs receive orientation
and training on how to prepare pre-feasibility studies, then prepare and submit their
pre-feasibility studies to the Board for screening. This process requires about 2.5
months and is completed in early April of the first year. The Board Secretariat then
holds a technical appraisal committee meeting to ensure that schemes meet Board
eligibility criteria (para. 3.26) to qualify schemes for site appraisal. Site appraisals
(including source yield measurement) of the selected schemes require another 2.5
months and are completed by mid-June of the first year.
*  Scheme Selection: The portfolio managers review the site appraisals, the technical
appraisal unit certifies the technical feasibility of the schemes, and a technical appraisal
committee meeting is held to propose a list of schemes that meet eligibility criteria for
final Board approval. This process requires about two months and is completed by
the third week of August of the first year.
*  Signing of development phase contract: After the Board approves the list of schemes,
the Board Secretariat starts a series of activities to facilitate SO preparation of
satisfactory development phase proposals. These activities consist of SOs training in
proposal preparation and screening and evaluating proposed SO staff, using a system
of orientation and training programs. The development phase contracts between the



- 20 -
Board and the SOs are then prepared and signed. These activities require about two
months and are completed by the end of October.
3.15     The development phase lasts about ten months and begins in November of the second
year. The cost of the development phase is about US$3,300 per community (equivalent to about
US$7.6 per capita). The development phase contract is a small contract of about US$16,500 per
SO (assuming that, on average, each SO works with five communities simultaneously), which
includes financing for staff, overheads, and two lump sums, one for the Sanitation Fund
(equivalent to about US$400 per scheme), the other to reimburse eligible SOs for their pre-
development phase costs (equivalent to about US$108 per scheme). For an average group of five
communities the SO will provide a team leader, a community development specialist who
supervises a community health worker, an overseer who supervises a community technician. The
main objective of the development phase is to prepare an implementation phase contract
agreement. The activities of the development phase are described in the terms of reference
attached to the development phase contract. The development phase include the following
activities:
*  Orientation and training for SO staff: This includes training on the preparation of a
community action plan to help beneficiary communities prepare an implementation
phase proposal, training for overseers, and training for the community technician on
household sanitation. This requires about one month and is completed by early
December of the second year.
*  Community action planning:  The community needs assessment is carried out.
Community development activities are carried out, including hygiene and sanitation
education (HSE), non-formal education (NFE), helping with legal registration of the
Water User Group, forming a representative Water User Committee, discussing
technical and service level options, and helping beneficiary communities to prepare
implementation phase proposals. Promotion and construction of household latrines is
started. These activities require about six months and are completed by mid-May of
the second year.
*  Finalization of implementation phase proposal: The community cash contribution is
collected, the community action plan is finalized, the detailed design is completed and
the implementation phase proposal is completed. The Board Secretariat carries out a
site appraisal, holds a technical appraisal committee meeting to review implementation
phase proposals' compliance with scheme eligibility criteria, and sends its
recommendation to the Board for final approval. These activities require about three
months and are completed by end-August of the second year.
3.16     The development phase would result in a registered Water User Group with a
representative Water User Committee (WUC), a community action plan which would form the
basis for the implementation phase proposal, community contributions to capital and operation



- 21 -
and maintenance costs, and, in cases which meet all Board eligibility criteria, a tripartite contract
agreement among the WUC, the Board and the SO for implementation phase financing.
3.17     The payment terms within the development phase contract have been set to permit
close monitoring of SO performance and early identification of problems. All payments are linked
to the completion of specific tasks. The first payment of 30% of the contract amount (equivalent
to about US$5,000 per SO) is conditional upon the signing of the development phase contract,
evidence that the SO has opened a separate bank account for the development phase activities,
evidence that all SO staff have been appointed, and receipt of a copy of the letter informing the
District Development Committee (DDC) concerned of the development phase activities.
3.18     The second payment of 40% of the contract amount (equivalent to about US$6,600
per SO) is conditional upon evidence that all HSE activities have been undertaken in accordance
with the terms of reference of the development phase, procedures for the use of the Sanitation
Fund have been established and agreed within the community, the Water User Group has opened
its own bank account, the Water User Group has been legally registered under the Water
Resources Act, a representative WUC has been formed, and the community resource map, the
layout plan, the baseline health data, the time-sheets of the SO field staff, and the WUC meeting
minute book are kept and maintained by the community.  In addition to its regular field
monitoring visits the Board hires an independent auditor to carry out an audit of the development
phase contract after the second payment is made.
3.19     The third payment of 30% of the total contract amount (equivalent to about US$5,000
per SO) is conditional upon evidence that the SO has submitted a satisfactory implementation
phase proposal, including all required community action plans and endorsed by the entire
community at a mass meeting, all HSE activities have been undertaken, the village maintenance
worker, the village health promoter, and the WUC treasurer have been selected, the Sanitation
Fund has been used satisfactorily, and the time-sheets of the SO staff, the WUC meeting minute
book, and the Water User Group account book are satisfactorily kept.
3.20     The Implementation phase costs about US$13,000 per community, of which about
US$2,550 is contributed by the community in cash and kind and about US$10,450 is contributed
by the Board, equivalent to a per capita cost of about US$30.0 (current population).  The
objective of this phase is to construct the water supply scheme, continue latrine construction, and
complete training of WJUC and community members. The implementation phase contract is a
tripartite agreement between the community, the SO, and the Board. The implementation phase
begins in August of the second year and lasts about 13 months. Signing of the implementation
phase contract is conditional upon evidence of a satisfactory audit of the development phase
(para. 3.18), deposit of the community cash contribution to the scheme construction cost in a joint
community/SO bank account, and deposit of the community cash contribution to the scheme's
first year's maintenance in the community bank account. The implementation phase includes the
following activities:



- 22 -
*  Training of SO staff and community members:  This includes the training of the
community technician, the village maintenance worker, the WIUC and tapstand groups,
and school teachers and community leaders.
*  Construction Planning:  This activity consist in the planning of the construction
activities, procurement of all materials and equipment and their transport to the
construction site. This activity requires about six weeks and is completed by mid
October of the second year.
*  Construction of the water supply scheme: All construction is carried out by the
communities in two stages with technical support and input from SOs and monitoring
by the Board. This activity requires about 6.5 months and is completed by early May
of the third year.
*  Sanitation: This activity consists of continuing to promote and construct household
and institutional latrines. It takes about five months and is completed by the end of
April of the third year.
-  Women's technical support services: This consists of an orientation on this program,
the carrying out of agreed upon activities and establishment of linkages with other
organizations. This activity requires about six weeks and is completed by early May of
the third year.
-  Source protection: This activity includes planting trees around the source area to
protect the source area from animal intrusion. It takes about four weeks and is
completed by mid-May of the third year.
3.21     The outcome of the implementation phase is the consolidation of all activities, a
completed and functioning water supply and sanitation schemes, and trained WlUC and
community members. The payment terms within the implementation phase contract have also
been set to closely monitor the performance of SOs and communities and identify problems early.
All payments are linked to the completion of well-defined tasks that can be monitored. During the
implementation phase two bank accounts are used. The first is a joint account operated by the
community and the SO, while the second is operated by the SO. The community and Board
contributions to the scheme's construction cost is deposited in the joint bank account, while the
Board contribution to the community development cost is deposited in the SO account. Three
payments are made to the SO account and two payments are made to the joint account as follows:
(1)  About 30% of the cost of community development activities is paid to the SO
account after the implementation phase contract is signed and all SO personnel
have been recruited. About 50% of the total Board contribution to scheme
construction costs (non-local materials, skilled labor, and transport) is paid to the
joint account upon submission of pro forma invoices for all non-local materials
for stage 1 of the construction.



- 23 -
(2)  About 40% of the total cost of community development activities is paid to the
SO account and about 50% of the total Board contribution to scheme
construction costs (non-local materials, skilled labor, and transport) is paid to the
joint account once: stage I of the scheme construction work has been carried
out satisfactorily; the non-local materials ordered have been delivered; all
community development activities (including HSE) have been undertaken; the
village maintenance worker is on the job; WUC construction management and
WUC treasurer training have been carried out; the Water User Group has
certified that all non-local materials have been delivered; scheme a'ccounts have
been kept; pro forma invoices have been received for all non-local materials for
stage 2 of construction; the Water User Group has certified that stage I
construction work has been completed; and  progress reports have been
submitted.
(3)  About 30% of the total cost of community development activities is paid to the
SO account upon evidence that: the scheme has been constructed in accordance
with contractual agreement; all community development activities are completed,
the village maintenance worker and the WUC have had refresher training;
complete scheme accounts have been submitted; and the community maintenance
fund has been replenished by the community.
3.22     To ensure economies of scale, single schemes would be avoided. Communities and
SOs would be encouraged to identify geographically clustered schemes for Board financing. Each
scheme would have site appraisals by the Secretariat during the pre-development and
development phases of the scheme cycle. The first site appraisal will take place in the pre-
development phase in the driest season (April - June) to confirm that appropriate yields are
available and to review scheme eligibility for development phase financing. The second will take
place during the development phase to review scheme eligibility for implementation phase
financing. From the pilot project, we assume a 13% dropout rate between the development and
implementation phases.
3.23     Two contractual agreements would be made, one for development phase financing
(between the Board and SO) and the other for implementation phase financing (between the
WUC, the Board, and the SO). The development phase contract would be signed at the end of the
pre-development phase, and the implementation phase contract at the end of the development
phase. The pre-development phase is not subject to any specific contract but its cost is paid to the
SO as a small lump sum  incorporated within the development phase contract.  During
negotiations, assurances were obtained that the Board will enter into arrangements with each SO
and communities under such terms and conditions as shall be acceptable to the Bank.
3.24     Support organization eligibility criteria.  The Board would select support
organizations according to the following criteria:



- 24 -
* Evidence of legal registration;
*  Constitutional provision to engage in rural water supply and sanitation service
delivery and related activities;
*  Audited and certified accounts;
*  Proven track record of at least two years in participatory rural water and sanitation
and/or related community development activities; and
*  Staffing capacity to carry out the proposed services.
3.25      Each SO  shall be further evaluated by the technical appraisal committee (TAC)
concerning its track record and staffing capacity using a ranking system which is described in the
Board's operations manual. The TAC will use this ranking system to recommend the number of
SOs it needs to undertake the corresponding batch of schemes. Support organization eligibility
criteria will be revised by the Board as needed in light of experience. In addition, the adequacy of
the criteria will be formally reviewed and assessed at the end of the implementation phase of each
batch of schemes. They would then be revised or modified to reflect lessons learned. During
negotiations agreement was obtained that Support Organization selection would be carried out in
accordance with the above eligibility criteria. Agreement was also obtained that the Board would
review the adequacy of the Support Organization eligibility criteria at the end of the
implementation phase of each batch of schemes and that changes introduced would need to be
acceptable to the Bank.
3.26      Scheme eligibility criteria. Each scheme accepted for Board financing would need
to meet eligibility criteria. These criteria would be applied at each phase in the cycle using
information available at that phase. During the initial operations of the Board, the three criteria of
need and economic viability, technical feasibility, and sustainability and willingness to pay would
apply. They are described below.
* Need and economic viability: this is satisfied if one or more of the following
condition holds:
(1)  The benefit/cost ratio is at least 1.5.8 This could be approximated at the pre-
development phase by asking whether the average time saved each day per
household would be more than 2 hours (for gravity schemes and deep
tubewells), 0.75 hours (for shallow tubewells), and 1.75 hours (for dugwells)
and whether estimated costs per capita were less than NRs 1,600 (for gravity
schemes), NRs 1,300 (for deep tubewells), NRs 230 (for shallow tubewells),
and NRs 1,000 (for dugwells).
8.     A benefit/cost ratio of 1.5 (rather than 1) is conservative and was chosen to allow some benefits to cover non-
quantified overhead costs of the Board, and to allow for contingencies.



- 25 -
(2) The average water availability is less than 15 liters per capita per day and
costs are below the thresholds in (1).
(3) A majority of households are dependent upon heavily polluted sources and a
scheme could provide cleaner water by protecting existing sources in the hills
or improving existing sources in the Terai and costs are below the thresholds
in (1).
*  Technical feasibility:  this is satisfied if the proposed source is undisputed,
unpolluted, and yields at least 45 liters per capita per day (if there is no other
solution, and existing sources are heavily polluted or provide under 15 lcd, a
minimum of 25 lcd would be accepted) and if the scheme meets the engineering
standards and service levels in the Board's technical guidelines and criteria,
including measures to mitigate adverse environmental consequences (Annex 15
and Annex 12).
*  Sustainability and willingness to pay is satisfied if all of the following conditions
hold:
(1) The community's contribution to the capital cost covers all unskilled labor,
locally available materials, portage, and all additional costs for service levels
higher than those provided under Board guidelines, and the cash contribution
is at least 2.5% of the cast construction cost in the hills, and 20% in the
Terai.9
(2) The community has made satisfactory operations and maintenance
arrangements.  These include engaging a village maintenance worker,
establishing systems for collecting maintenance funds on a regular basis, and
paying the first year's maintenance cost in advance (estimated at 3% of total
construction cost in the hills, and 4% in the Terai).
(3) A Water User Group has been formed and registered comprising all
beneficiary families, and a Water User Committee has been appointed to
manage the scheme, with representatives from each cluster and at least two
women.
(4) All beneficiaries who wish to participate are covered.
3.27      The sustainability and willingness to pay indicators would be appraised before the
development phase, and cash contributions would be deposited and the Water User Group
registered before the implementation contract was signed. The performance of schemes in
achieving these criteria would be measured again at the end of the project to ensure their
9.    Commnunity contributions in kind would be valued using the market rate for labor.



- 26 -
continued robustness and applicability. The Board will revise or modify the scheme eligibility
criteria as needed to reflect lessons of experience, especially at the end of the implementation
phase of each batch of schemes, when they will be formally reviewed and assessed. During
negotiations agreement was obtained that Scheme selection would be carried out in accordance
with the above eligibility criteria. Agreement was also obtained that the Board would review the
adequacy of the Scheme eligibility criteria at the end of the implementation phase of each batch of
schemes and that changes introduced would need to be acceptable to the Bank.
3.28     Role of women. Women are the main users and managers of water resources, and
influence family sanitary habits through their central role in family hygiene, child care, and food
preparation. As women are the ones who fetch water, they value improvements in water supplies
more than men and so have a vested interest in keeping the system functioning. Carrying water is
one of the most arduous tasks in rural areas of Nepal and is done primarily by women and female
children. Women are responsible for all household chores related to water, such as collecting and
storing water, taking care of sources and distribution points, and working to construct them.
Women also decide whether or not to use a specific water point, given its distance and
accessibility. They do not participate much in development, however, because household
activities take up too much time, there is a low level of awareness, and they have limited control
over resources. Despite these constraints, programs in Nepal and elsewhere have shown that
women could be more involved in initiating and managing their own water supply systems.
Hence, project implementation strategy would focus on strengthening the role of women in all
stages of scheme cycle to increase their capacity and to ensure they fully participate in project
benefits. A social assessment has been carried out and is presented in Annex 16.
3.29     The community development activities of the Water Supply and Sanitation Services
component will promote the role of women in five key areas. Women will be guaranteed
representation in Water User Committees, which much have at least two women members; their
selection by women's groups will be promoted. Hygiene and sanitation and non-formal education
activities will focus on women. Women will be encouraged to take advantage of the women's
technical support services (WTSS) program, which would provide specific skill and management
training to increase the scope of income generating activities, and would help women gain access
to formal credit systems. Women will be encouraged to form female tapstand groups to collect
monthly operation and maintenance fees and to maintain tapstands. The selection of female
scheme maintenance workers will be encouraged. Women will be trained in operation and
maintenance, including collection of user fees, monitoring and reporting.

3.31      Selection &  construction of water supply and sanitation schemes.  The total
estimated cost of water supply and sanitation schemes for the project would be about US$15.23
million, inclusive of contingencies, taxes, and duties. About 1,035 communities (assuming a 13%
dropout rate between the development and implementation phases) would be contracted for
development phase, resulting in 207 development phase contracts (for an average value of about
US$16,600 per contract and assuming that each support organization, on average, will
simultaneously work with five communities). About 900 implementation phase contracts - one
contract per community with an average value of US$13,000, of which about US$2,000 is for
community development activities and US$1 1,000 is for scheme construction - would be
required. Each development phase contract would be primarily for community development
activities, while each implementation phase contract would include non-local materials (small
diameter pipes, fittings, taps, handpumps, cement, etc.), community mobilization and
participation, labor, portage, and locally available materials. These numerous small contracts
would be scattered over a large number of small communities, and spread over the five years'
implementation period. Because beneficiary communities will contribute cash, all unskilled labor,
portage, and locally available materials, competitive procurement procedures may be impractical.
Support organizations would be recruited as service providers under competitive bidding
whenever possible, and otherwise as sole providers. The Board would ensure efficiency and



- 28 -
economy by comparing a support organization's cost proposal with a schedule of standard unit
costs and prices developed under the pilot project, which it would update from time to time.
3.32     Goods and equipment for the Board.  The total estimated cost of goods and
equipment to be procured for the Board would be about US$0.34 million, inclusive of
contingencies, duties, and taxes. The only goods and equipment to be procured for the Board are
vehicles, computers, office furniture, and miscellaneous equipment. These cannot be grouped into
packages of more than US$50,000; they can only be grouped into several homogenous contracts,
to be procured under a separate time schedule, each estimated to cost less than the equivalent of
US$50,000. For this reason it is proposed that goods and equipment be procured by the Board
on the basis of prudent shopping.
3.33     Technical assistance, training, studies, audits, monitoring, publicity, and water
quality testing. The total estimated cost of these items for the project would be about US$4. 18
million, inclusive of contingencies and duties. Consultants paid by the Bank would be selected
following Bank guidelines.
3.34     Contract review. All individual contracts for goods and equipment for the Board and
water supply and sanitation schemes estimated to cost the equivalent of US$50,000 or more
would be subject to the Bank's prior review and approval. In addition, the Bank will review all
first batch contracts for water supply and sanitation schemes. Contracts for consulting firms in
excess of US$100,000 equivalent (for individual consultants, in excess of US$50,000 equivalent)
would be subject to the Bank's prior review and approval. Agreement to the above was obtained
during credit negotiations. This will allow prior review of contracts representing at least 10% of
the procurement under the project.
Disbursements
3.35     On the basis of the proposed implementation schedule (Annex 7), disbursements
would continue for five and a half years, less than the nine years' standard disbursement profile
for Nepal. This shorter disbursement profile is suitable for a program of investments, each of
which has a maximum duration of 36 months. It was also adopted to take into account the pilot
project's identification of a pipeline of schemes for Board financing. Disbursement is therefore
expected to be faster than normal for the first year of project implementation. The proceeds of
the Credit would be disbursed, net of taxes and duties, as outlined in Table 3.2 below.

3.36      Water supply and sanitation schemes include two contracts, one for the development
phase and the other for the implementation phase. The development phase contract provides
funding for support organizations, including the cost of employing a team leader, a community
development specialist, a community health worker, an overseer, and a community technician, and
pre-development phase costs, and overheads, as well as funding for household latrine construction
under the Sanitation Fund. The implementation phase contract specifies the total amount, how
much will be contributed by the community and how much will be contributed by the Board. The
Board's contribution at the implementation phase includes funding for support organization staff
and overheads as above, institutional latrine construction, non-local materials and equipment, and
women's technical support services.   Non-statutory audits will be internal audits and
scheme/support organization technical audits will be carried out by a private audit firm recruited
by the Board. Statements of expenditure would be used for disbursements against expenditures



- 30 -
on contracts of US$50,000 or less for: i) goods, works and services for the schemes; ii)
equipment and materials for the Board; iii) Board operating costs; and iv) individual consultants
for services and training and monitoring and audits (other than statutory audits) and publicity and
water quality testing. Statements of expenditure would also be used for disbursements against
expenditures on contracts for consulting firms of US$100,000 or less.  The corresponding
documentation would not be submitted to the Bank but would be retained by the Board and made
available for inspection by auditors and during the course of Bank project supervision missions.
For all other expenditures, disbursements would be fully documented.  During negotiations
agreement was obtained that Board adoption of an implementation manual and rules satisfactory
to the Bank would be a condition for disbursement for schemes. The credit closing date is March
31, 2002. A schedule of disbursements is shown in Annex 8.
3.37     Project Preparation Advance and Special account. A Project Preparation Advance
of US$528,000 has been approved in June 1996 to complete project preparation. To facilitate
and expedite disbursements, a special account with an authorized allocation of US$0.8 million
(representing about four months of Bank-financed expenditures) would be established by the
Board on terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank. In particular, the Borrower would
designate the Board's Executive Director as its representative to operate the special account.
Agreement to this effect was obtained during credit negotiations. The special account would be
used to finance all eligible expenditures, and would be replenished from time to time upon receipt
and approval of withdrawal applications supported by the required documentation.
Accounts. Audit, and Reportin2
3.38     Accounts. A project accounting and record keeping system for the Board has been
developed by the pilot project. This system meets the accounting and reporting requirements of
the government as well as the Board's accounting requirements. The project accounting system
records:
.  Expenditures for establishment and operation of the Rural Water Supply and
Sanitation Fund Development Board;
.  Expenditures for selection and construction of rural water supply and sanitation
schemes, with a breakdown on a scheme basis between development phase and
implementation phase costs;
*  Expenditures for institutional development and studies.
3.39     A scheme accounting manual has also been developed for support organizations
(SOs). Each SO would be required in its contract to maintain separate scheme accounts and
records to support the Board scheme accounting system. The SO would have to provide a
detailed breakdown of expenditures on a scheme basis for each main item, categorized into
community contribution (labor, portage, and locally available materials), other itemized
construction costs, and itemized community development costs. Assurances were obtained,



- 31 -
during credit negotiations, that the Board would require SOs to keep separate accounts for each
scheme and to adopt and follow the above accounting system. Each SO would be required to
regularly provide the Board with updated actual scheme accounts on expenditures. Receipt of
such information by the Board would be a condition of further Board disbursements to an SO.
Assurances to this effect were obtained during credit negotiations. In particular, signing of the
implementation phase contract will be conditional upon receipt of a satisfactory audit report,
prepared by an independent commercial auditor recruited by the Board, of the expenditures
undertaken until the second payment under the development phase contract. Assurances to this
effect were obtained during credit negotiations.
3.40     Audit. The Board would maintain records and accounts in accordance with sound
accounting practices to reflect the operations, resources, and all expenditures in respect of the
project. Such records and accounts of the Board (including statements of expenditure and the
special account) would be audited annually (statutory audit) by the Kingdom of Nepal's Auditor
General's office in accordance with appropriate auditing principles, consistently applied. The
Board would be required to send the Bank, no later than three months after the close of its fiscal
year, a copy of its unaudited financial statements and, no later than nine months after the close of
its fiscal year, a copy of its audited financial statements and audit reports. Any such other
information concerning the said records, accounts, and the audit, as may be reasonably requested
by the Bank, would be furnished by the Board. In addition to the foregoing, the Board would, in
respect of withdrawals made on the basis of statements of expenditure, retain all records
evidencing such expenditures for at least one year after the Bank receives the audit report for the
fiscal year in which the last withdrawal is made. Such records would be made available to the
Bank's representatives on request and would be included in the annual audit reports. The audit
report would contain an opinion by the auditors as to whether the statements of expenditure
submitted, and the procedures and internal controls involved in their preparation, could be relied
upon to support the related withdrawals. Agreement was obtained, to this effect during credit
negotiations. In addition, scheme accounts kept by support organizations would be subject to
audits carried out by independent auditors to be recruited by the Board in consultation with the
Bank. Such audits would take place after the second payment of the development phase contract
and after completion of the implementation phase contract. The terms of reference of the
commercial auditors would include a requirement that they carry out a separate audit of the
efficiency and effectiveness of the Board's procurement function. Agreement was obtained, to
this effect, during credit negotiations.
3.41     Flow of funds. The Board Secretariat would prepare annual budgets for Board
approval. Once approved, the annual budgets would be forwarded to the Ministry of Housing
and Physical Planning for transmission to the National Planning Commission (NPC) and the
Ministry of Finance. It was agreed during project preparation and appraisal that upon final
approval by parliament, the Ministry of Finance would make available on a grant basis such
amounts as shall be required by the Board for its operations. This agreement was confirmed
during credit negotiations. The  Board would release funding to support organizations and
communities in accordance with the payment terms of the development phase and implementation
phase contractual agreements (paras 3.17 - 3.19 and 3.21).



- 32 -
Monitorine. Evaluation and SuRervision
3.42     Monitoring and evaluation play a critical role in ensuring both that the overall profile
of Board performance is in line with the aims and objectives, and that the implementation of
specific projects facilitated by the Board incorporate the required level of technical quality and
effective beneficiary participation to make them sustainable. Because the Board is set up as a
facilitating instrument rather than as a delivery system with its own line of execution, the
monitoring and evaluation also has to ensure that adequate information is provided to all the
relevant stakeholders and managers - the community, the Water User Committee, support
organizations, the Board, and the Bank- to enable each to exercise their respective management
and supervisory functions.
3.43     The Board will activate and rely on a network of independent support organizations
to provide technical and financial support to isolated and weakly organized communities wanting
to improve and manage their own water supply and sanitation facilities.  The operational
procedures need to allow for a judicious balance between flexibility and prescription. Monitoring
and evaluation must therefore enable the Board to strike the right balance between the necessary
risks inherent in this approach on one hand, and the need for enforcing compliance with standards
and rules on the other.
3.44     Monitoring and evaluation will differ between the Board (or program) level and the
scheme level. Because the Board is a facilitating agency, its has to monitor the overall progress
and impact of the investments made, and the quality and performance of the contracted delivery
system.  With respect to the specific schemes the primary managers are the Water User
Committees and the support organizations. Monitoring and evaluation must consequently address
their responsibilities and interests in terms of field implementation and quality control.
Contractual compliance will be the Board's concern.
3.45     A comprehensive Monitoring and evaluation system is laid down for the Board with
five distinct points:
*  Compliance. Contracts between the Board, support organizations, and Water User
Committees will be the main point of reference. Checking compliance involves routine
and standardized follow-up (including audits) of financial management, agreed
activities, and quality of outputs. Its purpose is to trigger corrective actions or
sanctions to ensure compliance with core operational requirements.
*  Process/development. This will focus on how the quality and thrust of the services
provided change over time, and will involve a continuous assessment of how roles and
responsibilities are played out over the scheme cycle as well as over batches. Its
purpose is to enable revisions of the Board's procedural frameworks and instruments.



- 33 -
Community. This_will focus on users' capacity to manage the assets and awareness
generated by participation in the Board program. Support organizations will facilitate
community assessments of the need for and quality of services and facilities provided.
The purpose is to provide an informed basis for active community management and
involvement.
* Impact. Changes generated by the activities, inputs, and services will be assessed in
terms of the long-term objectives of the Board. Post-implementation community
management of assets and the benefits (health, time savings, etc.) likely to accrue on a
sustained basis will be assessed. The purpose is to provide the Board, and the overall
sector policy-makers, with a continuous assessment of the economic, financial, and
social justification for the program of investment.
*  Strategy.   This is geared to documenting, disseminating, and learning from
experience. The Board's adaptive and programmatic approach requires feedback on
which approaches are working well and which are not.  This feedback can be
incorporated into the project in a cyclic or programmatic way.
3.46      The above issues would be monitored through: regular visits by Board and service
agency staff; scheme completion reports prepared by support organizations and service agencies
or the Board; audits by independent auditors; scheme Completion Reports to be written by the
Board after one season of operation for a sample of schemes; Knowledge, Attitudes, and
Practices studies before and after schemes for a selected number of communities each year; and
reviews of a small sample of schemes four years after completion to assess impact and
sustainability issues in particular.
3.47      The Board's monitoring and evaluation indicators focus both on scheme-specific
issues over the scheme cycle, and program -level issues. Scheme-specific issues include the rate
and quality of implementation, the performance and capacity of support organizations and Water
user Committees, the extent to which assumed benefits are being realized, cost-efficiency, and the
degree of active management by the community.  The program-level issues include cost-
effectiveness of service delivery through the private sector, suitability of Board policies and
procedures (including support organization and scheme selection criteria), impact of water and
sanitation interventions under varying socioeconomic and environmental conditions, and relative
efficiency of different technology options and design criteria. Monitoring indicators developed for
the Board are shown in Annex 9.
3.48      The Secretariat of the Board will have a limited in-house capacity to pursue the
monitoring and evaluation undertaken (para. 3.05). The Operations/Monitoring and Evaluation
Unit head would coordinate overall monitoring and evaluation. In addition, the Board will
contract service agencies, institutionally or individually, to carry  out the necessary field
investigations and analysis.



- 34 -
3.49      The National Planning Commission and the Ministry of Housing and physical Planning
will be provided with technical assistance to enable them to monitor and evaluate the overall
sector performance, and compare different delivery systems, from the public and private sectors.
Experts on short-term contracts would do this work by studying a sample of projects in different
parts of the country and under different programs. With reliable and systematic information being
made available to the planning authorities independently of the delivery system, policy-makers
could make better decisions about project and inter-agency resource allocations.
3.50      The Board shall prepare yearly work programs as well as quarterly and annual
progress reports. The reports shall cover the progress of the Board operations, including physical
progress, costs, disbursements, technical assistance, training, and other pertinent information,
together with results of monitoring and evaluation of processes, impacts, and lessons learnt. A
copy of the draft annual work program shall be submitted to the Bank for its review and
comments two months before the beginning of the fiscal year. Copies of the quarterly and annual
progress reports shall be submitted to the Bank within one month of the end of each quarter and
within two months of the end of each fiscal year, respectively.  The reports shall highlight
constraints as well as proposed remedies, based on the results of the monitoring and evaluation.
Assurances to this effect were obtained during credit negotiations.
3.51      The project is expected to require about 22 staff weeks of implementation supervision
for its first year, 1 7 for its second year, about 21 staff weeks for its third year (mid-term review)
and 14 staff weeks thereafter, in total, about 88 staff weeks over the five-year implementation
period. This estimate is based on the fact that this is the first Bank-financed project in Rural
Water Supply and Sanitation in Nepal. In addition, the project's finance of dispersed investments
will face logistical challenges, and some support organizations will be dealing with a new
approach to community participation in decision making. The supervision plan is presented in
Annex 10.
3.52      A mid-term project evaluation will be carried out no later than June 30, 1999 (at the
end of the third year). The Kingdom of Nepal and the Board would review progress and decide
whether components, implementation arrangements, policy reforms, and Board operating
procedures need adjusting, as defined in the Board implementation manual. This mid-term review
will provide a forum between the Kingdom of Nepal, the Board and the Bank to discuss issues
identified and agree on remedial measures and will be followed by the remedial actions agreed
upon. Assurances to this effect were obtained during credit negotiations.
IV. BENEFITS AND RISKS
Willingness to Pay. Affordability, and Poverty Alleviation
4.01      Households under this project would be expected to make a substantial initial
contribution to capital cost, and then to mobilize funds for all subsequent operations and
maintenance expenditures. The initial contribution to capital cost would comprise, for all water
schemes, the unskilled labor, locally available materials, and portage. In addition, communities



- 35 -
would contribute a proportion of the cash costs; 2.5% of the cost of gravity schemes in the hills,
and 20% of the cost of various well schemes in the Terai. The total cash and non-cash capital
contributions would be about 40% of the cost of most schemes. Communities would also make
an initial cash deposit equal to the average estimated annual operations and maintenance
expenditure (3% of scheme construction cost in the hills, 4% in the Terai) which would be
managed by the Water User Committee.
4.02      The initial cash contributions to capital and the operations and maintenance fund
would come to NRs 273 for shallow tubewells and NRs 830 for deep tubewells per household.
Total contributions would range from NRs 503 for shallow tubewells to NRs 2,924 for gravity
schemes per household. Only in some cases would the required contribution be more than two
months' income for a household in the lowest decile; schemes would thus be affordable (Table
4.1). This was also true of the pilot project. Under the first pilot batch of schemes, communities
contributed NRs 379 per capita (38% of scheme costs) and in the second pilot batch, they
contributed NRs 440 per capita (40% of scheme costs) for gravity schemes. These figures are
equivalent to NRs 2,31 1 and NRs 2,684 per household for gravity schemes. The contributions
proposed for the project are comparable.
Table 4.1: Monthly rural household income, 1996
NRs per household per month
Terai                     Hills
Cash      Total           Cash       Total
Lowest dccile    500       1,600           275       1,240
Median         1,(1(      2,850           1,095      2,560
Avcrage         1,500     3,700           1,100      3,230
4.03      The percentage cash contribution to capital cost was initially proposed to induce
communities to seek low-cost designs. Limited experiments in the Terai indicate that substantial
economies may be achieved if communities are supported on a matching grant basis and fully
understand the technology choices.
4.04      If a community is willing to pay, the question arises as to whether full cost-recovery
should be attempted. The payment per household to recover all capital and operations and
maintenance costs (assuming a discount rate of 10% in real terms) would be between NRs 34 per
month for shallow tubewells and NRs 111 per month for gravity schemes. This represents
between 3% and 10% of monthly household cash income, and 1% to 4% of total income
respectively (compare Annex I 1, Tables I and 9) Such amounts are affordable in principle; the
problem would be securing repayments from communities once systems were installed, given their
geographical location and the seasonal irregularity of rural incomes. Past failures in Nepal's urban
areas to recover even partial costs at negative real interest rates suggest the unfeasibility of cost
recovery efforts, as contrasted with the proposed up-front capital contributions.



- 36 -
4.05     Mobilizing all the community resources required to support operations and
maintenance (O&M) on a continuous basis is not as well documented as raising initial
contributions. The continued operation of NGO-sponsored schemes after many years indicates
that community O&M is feasible, however. Several communities surveyed have said they are
willing to pay NRs 13 - 30 per household per month (1993 prices) for water supplies. These
figures are comparable to estimated O&M costs of the most expensive schemes. The initial O&M
costs for gravity schemes would come to about NRs 19 per household per month, and about NRs
26 for deep tubewell (see Annex 11, Tables 3 to 6 for details).
4.06     Poverty Alleviation. Although cost estimates, income statistics, and survey results all
imply that schemes were affordable, some poor households could probably not contribute equally
to costs. Even if this were the case, a condition for scheme support would be that all households
would have access to the facilities (see the Board Rules, Annex 6). The pilot project showed that
communities will care for their weaker members, while bringing sanctions to bear on households
which are able to pay but hope to benefit as freeloaders.
4.07     We do not plan to target poor communities explicitly in the hills. Many NGOs seek
out less favored communities for assistance, implying that some automatic antipoverty targeting is
built into the demand-led Board procedures. Richer communities have often already benefited
from public or private schemes. Many poor families are members of larger communities, rather
than being geographically isolated in communities of their own. Since complete coverage within
communities is an eligibility criterion, poor people should benefit at least in proportion to their
share of the total population.  Taking these factors together, the proportion of  project
beneficiaries who are poor will probably be more than 40 - 60% (the proportion of all rural
households which are poor, depending on the definition of poverty). In the Terai, on the other
hand, private and public programs have resulted in widespread coverage, but may have missed
some groups, such as landless tenants. The pilot project could not get enough information on this
issue to recommend ways to ensure that the Board reaches the most undeserved groups in the
Terai. The project therefore includes a study on Terai strategy to be carried out in the first
project year.
Environmental Assessment
4.08     The project is expected to have a net positive effect on the environment. Rural water
supply and sanitation schemes are expected to save women and children's time and energy
because there will be less need for them to fetch water, improving family health and giving women
opportunities to use the time saved productively. Water will be used more effectively for bathing,
washing, and cleaning. Rural women are expected to have fewer children because of reduced
death and disease rates for children and babies. Hygiene and sanitation practices, and family
health are expected to improve. The environment will be less contaminated because of controlled
disposal of human wastes, and environmental management and catchment protection are expected
to improve. Finally, communities will be more self-reliant and independent.



- 37 -
4.09     Rural water supply and sanitation schemes may also have potential negative
environmental impacts. Reservoirs or other components may overflow and cause erosion and
waterlogging. Increased water use without adequate wastewater disposal may cause pollution
problems. Inappropriate sanitation units (latrines) or poorly built wells may pollute groundwater.
If there is no effective treatment, bringing contaminated surface water closer to households and
using polluted sources may have adverse effects on health. Increasing domestic water use may
deny future users its agricultural and other uses. Expansion of settlement areas because of
improved water supplies may lead to congestion and environmental pollution. Finally, quarrying
and use of access roads may cause erosion where undertaken. Many of the potential adverse
impacts will be mitigated or avoided by activities planned under the project. Notwithstanding
these potentially adverse environmental problems, the net environmental impact of the project is
expected to be positive. Annex 12 describes potential environmental impacts and mitigation
measures of the project in more detail.
Promotion of the Private Sector
4.10     A major objective of this project is for the government to stop providing those services
which are more efficiently provided by the private sector, and to focus its attention on activities
which it must carry out. These activities, in the case of rural water supply and sanitation, include
subsidizing private provision where appropriate, in this case through Board grants to support
organizations and communities, monitoring the performance of different sector actors in
accomplishing sectoral objectives (which would be sought through the institutional development
and studies component); carrying out research and development on appropriate technologies; and
improving policies to facilitate efficient service delivery. The most important immediate impacts
on the private sector would arise from the funding of schemes started and carried out with the
help of NGOs and communities. Training the staff of NGOs, community-based organizations and
other private groups under the project would also enhance the capacity of the private sector to
carry out expanded future programs. The Board would not only help the government to play a
smaller role under decentralized delivery of water supply and sanitation services, but would also
provide useful precedents for other sectors.
Impacts on Women
4.11     The project would give women and children more access to improved, adequate, and
safe water and sanitation facilities, which in turn would benefit their health and productivity.
Time and energy would be saved, and improved health would increase productivity. Women
would have more privacy, brought about by improved sanitation facilities, and they would play a
bigger role in managing water supply and sanitation schemes, including water regulation,
collection of user fees, maintenance, and reporting, monitoring, and evaluation. The non-formal
education and technical support services to women would increase literacy.
4.12     The project activities designed for the participation of women would instill a sense of
confidence through increased mobility and interaction around the community and increase access
to productive activities. This would result in institutional and social benefits such as Water User
Groups that function well, and enhanced status of women.



- 38 -
4.13      The negative attitude towards more active participation of women in non-traditional
activities may prove more difficult to overcome and result in lower participation rates than
expected. Such negative attitudes would be addressed through specific information and education
awareness programs targeting local authorities, community leaders, and the community at large.
Economic Analysis
4.14      Project justification. The proposed project would be justified on the basis of its
direct benefits to about 400,000 rural inhabitants initially, rising to about 550,000 as village
populations grow to design levels. As noted in para. 4.10 above, it would also generate non-
quantifiable benefits in the long term through the continued operations of the institutions
established under the project. In the longer term, many more communities would benefit from the
future operations of the Board, whose initial operations would be financed by the project.
Project-funded research and development activities would result in improved practices and
techniques for cost-effective supply of water and sanitation services. By developing institutions
and procedures for public funding of demand-led community-based development, this project
would enable wider replication of sustainable rural water and sanitation facilities than has been
possible using private - or public - funds alone.
4.15      Direct benefits. Rural populations would save time collecting water, and be healthier
because of more and cleaner water, improved health knowledge, and better hygiene practices.
The only benefits which have been quantified are the value of time saved in water collection and
the value of increased water consumed due to lower collection costs. No quantification of future
health benefits has been attempted because there is not enough information.
4.16      Time saving in the hills. Most schemes supported by the project are in the hills
(gravity schemes). The estimates of time saved and increased water use are based on three
different sources: a study for UNICEF by the All India Institute of Public Health (AIIPH; see
Annex 20, AIIPH 1992), Knowledge, Attitudes and Practices (KAP) studies carried out before
and after nine schemes in the pilot project (six in the hills, three in the Terai); and baseline and
impact data collected for 35 pilot project schemes as part of normal processing. Each source
provides different information and is subject to substantial uncertainty, but together they provide a
basis for robust estimates about schemes in the hills. According to the AIIrPH study, the average
household served by a gravity system increased its water consumption (for household purposes
alone) from 19 to 32 liters per capita per day, while the time cost of collecting water decreased
from 2.68 minutes per liter to 0.92 minutes per liter. This implies that the demand for water has
an arc elasticity of -0. 5, which is within the range found in intemational studies (elasticities of -0.2
to -0.7). The average benefit from cost saving on the initial level of consumption would be 184
minutes per day per average household of 5.5 people, while the consumer' surplus on incremental
consumption would be 63 minutes.  According to the KAP studies, the water carried by
households (for human consumption and cattle) rose from 37.8 to 39.3 liters per capita per day,
while the unit time cost of water collection fell from 1. 18 minutes per liter to 0.48 minutes per
liter, implying a rather low arc elasticity of -0.05. The average benefit from cost saving on the



- 39 -
initial level of consumption would be 158 minutes per day per household of 6 people, and the
benefit from increased water carried would be 3.1 minutes. The data from the 35 completed pilot
schemes showed that the average water collection trip was 38.8 minutes long before the scheme
and 7.4 minutes long after the scheme. The time saved per trip was 31.4 minutes, which, for an
average of 10.5 water collection trips per day, implied savings on the initial level of water
consumption of 337 minutes. Estimates of initial trip times may be high side because of a
tendency to exaggerate potential time savings and secure a scheme, but the number of trips may
be underestimated. Data on water collection for cattle were excluded in many communities, while
the KAP studies found that collecting water for cattle sometimes took as many trips as water for
household consumption. For purposes of the base economic rate of return, the time saved in the
hills is estimated to be 170 minutes per household per day, and the value of increased
consumption at 20% of this, or 34 minutes per day.
4.17      Time saving in the Terai. Evidence on time saving in the Terai is less reliable and
complete than for the hills. Household surveys for the Ministry of Health suggest that improved
shallow tubewells save 4 minutes per round trip for each 12-liter container, and increase
consumption about as much as schemes in the hills. An average household of 6 people would
save about 35 minutes per day, and the value of incremental consumption would be about 9
minutes. On the other hand, the KAP studies for three pilot project schemes showed a reduction
of only 0.03 minutes per liter, and a reduction in the amount of water carried and consumed per
capita and per household, even taking account of cattle consumption.'° For purposes of the base
economic rate of return, the time saved by shallow tubewells are estimated to be 30 minutes per
household per day, and induced consumption at 6 minutes per day. These do not yield the
threshold benefit/cost ratio of 1.5 (see para. 4.26) but some schemes will probably be required to
replace heavily polluted sources. Deep tubewells and dugwells are appropriate in areas with
lower groundwater tables where shallow tube wells are not feasible, and the time spent fetching
water in such areas is more typical of the hills than the Terai. On the assumption that the Board
applies selection criteria rigorously, but allowing for some schemes on the grounds of shortage of
water or the need to replace heavily polluted sources, it is estimated that these deep tubewells and
dugwells would save 60 minutes per household per day and give 12 minutes' worth of additional
water.
4.18      Value of time saved. The valuation of time saved is based on the use of time saved
found by the AIIPH study. That study found that 30% of time saved was devoted to economic
activities, 16% to household activities (child-rearing, housekeeping, etc.), and the remainder to
socializing, sleeping, etc. The value of these activities has been estimated at 100% of the average
rural wage for economic activities, 50% for household activities, and 25% for other activities.
This is consistent with practice in other demand studies and in transport studies. The average
rural wage is estimated at NRs 40 per day. The value of time saved in gravity schemes would
thus come to NRs 2,660 per household per year, and the value of increased water consumed to
NRs 530 per capita per year (Table 3 of Annex 11). Together, these benefits would amount to
10.   The statistical uncertainties of these data are underlined by the fact that the estimate of persons per household went
from 6.31 to 5.14 between the before and after surveys in the same communities.



- 40 -
about NRs 3,196, or more than the median monthly income in the hills." The benefits for the
other types of households would be lower, due to lower time savings.
4.19       Costs. The cost estimates are based on the pilot project. Community development
costs for each type of scheme include the development costs of the 13% of communities which
drop out between development and implementation phases. The estimates are summarized in
Annex 1 1.
4.20       Benefit/cost analysis and economic rates of return. Benefits and costs have been
forecast over a 24-year horizon from the first year of the project in 1996 border prices. Border
prices have been estimated by applying the standard conversion factor of 0.9 to all domestic
expenditures and benefits. Switching values have been calculated using the 10% opportunity cost
of capital for Nepal. Economic rates of return (ERRs) and switching values have been calculated
for each type of scheme, first including all community development as well as construction
expenses, and then including only construction costs. The first estimate understates ERRs, since
there is no quantification of health benefits.  The second overstates ERRs, since costs of
community mobilization and education are excluded. Economic rates of return are then calculated
for all schemes together according to the assumed mix of scheme types (Annex 11, Tables 2 and
3). Finally, a total project ERR is estimated by adding to the scheme costs all other project costs
(such as Board operating costs, institutional development, and studies).  Since there is no
allowance for benefits of these non-scheme expenditures, and no accounting for scheme health
benefits, the estimated project ERR of 15% is conservative. The ERR for schemes alone comes
to 22% and, for construction alone, to about 31%. The total project would result in a net present
value of NRs 218.0 million, the net present value for schemes alone would be NRs 396.0 million
and the net present value for construction alone would be NRs 511.0 million. A summary of
results is in Table 4.2 and details are provided in Annex 11. Table 4.3 sets out implications for
scheme and project ERRs on different risk scenarios described below.
