4.21      Perhaps the major economic risk in this project is that some schemes would provide no
benefits following completion of all investments. The consequences of 20% of schemes failing
outright would be approximated by the "benefits - 20%" scenario. The switching values for total
project benefits indicate that fully 25% of schemes would have to fail from the start to reduce the
project ERR to 10%. More than 25% are unlikely to fail, given the analysis provided in para.
4.22. Assuming that all schemes failed after ten years, the ERR on schemes would fall to 17%
and project ERR would fall to 8%. The very large benefits from well-selected gravity schemes
should exceed the relatively conservative base line assumptions, however.
Table 4.3: Economic rates of return under different scenarios
Scenario                                    All schemes          Total project
Base case                                    22%                  15%
20% of schemes fail (benefits - 20%)         17%                  11%
Benefits + 20%                               27%                  18%
Worst Case: costs + 20%, benefits - 20%      14%                   9%
All schemes fail after ten years             17%                  8%
Risks
4.22      The two principal risks of the project would be that more time and effort than now
foreseen might be required to build capacity in NGOs, communities, and the Board itself, implying
a shortfall in implementation from the current forecasts, and that outside pressures might prevent
the Board from allocating resources in a transparent fashion according to the objective criteria
agreed with the Bank. The adverse consequences of the first possibility would be minimized by
several elements of project design. First, the extensive pilot project has developed workable
procedures for rigorously applying the eligibility criteria, capacity building for support
organizations and communities, and monitoring scheme implementation.  Second, advisory
services during the initial years would help to overcome institutional teething problems and
provide on-the-job training to Board staff. Third, the number of schemes will grow gradually,
partly because the relationship between seasons and project cycles means that no hills schemes are
scheduled for implementation in the second year. This allows for delays and for learning from the
first batch of schemes without major consequences for the overall schedule. Fourth, since the
project would finance a program of schemes which met agreed eligibility criteria, disbursements
would be accelerated, slowed, or discontinued depending on performance of support
organizations and communities. The rate of implementation would be self-regulating. Fifth,
basing time saving criteria upon a benefit/cost ratio of 1.5, rather than 1.0, allows for risk and also
ensures that the project generates returns to cover Board overheads. Sixth, competence and
motivation of Board staff would be maximized by engaging them through open competition on
contract terms, with remuneration levels competitive with the private sector, and making their
tenure dependent upon performance. The pilot project has shown that such procedures work in
Nepal. The other risk, of outside political pressure, is more problematic. As noted above,
inadequate autonomy in public bodies is a major source of poor performance. Measures to
address this could obviously not be tested under the pilot project. The Board Formation Order



- 42 -
provides for the minimum degree of autonomy required for the Board to control the rules which
determine eligibility criteria and terms for staff engagement. The principal way to mitigate this
risk is to allocate substantial resources to monitoring and evaluation and to Bank supervision.
Finally, the Kingdom of Nepal has agreed to a mid-term review (para. 3.52), at which time the
project could be restructured, discontinued, or accelerated as necessary in the light of experience.
V. AGREEMENTS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
5.01     At negotiations, the following assurances were obtained:
a) The DWSS's service delivery responsibilities would be phased out no later than July
16, 1997 for the Central and Western Development Regions of Nepal, no later than
July 16, 1998 for the Eastern Development Region of Nepal and no later than July 16,
1999 for the Mid-Western and Far Western Development Regions of Nepal for
communities with a population of less than 500 that are funded by the Kingdom of
Nepal's own resources (para. 2.05);
b) The detailed demand study and the study for the identification of criteria for selection
of schemes in the Terai would be carried out in accordance with terms of reference
agreed with the Bank. These two studies would be completed by March 31, 1999 and
June 30, 1998 respectively and study findings would be discussed with the Bank
within 6 months thereafter and an action plan prepared and implemented (para 2.06);
c) The Kingdom of Nepal shall take all action necessary on its part to enable the Board
to formulate and implement principles and procedures for the recruitment, employment
and compensation of its staff (para 3 .03);
d) A qualified technical advisor would be recruited by the Board before January 1, 1997
(para 3.08);
e) The Kingdom of Nepal will make the proceeds of the Credit available to the Board,
under a grant agreement to be entered into between the Kingdom of Nepal and the
Board, under terms and conditions which shall be satisfactory to the Bank, including
programs requesting the Board to utilize the proceeds of the Credit to provide grants
to develop and implement rural water supply and sanitation schemes in accordance
with the provisions of the Formation Order, the rules and the implementation manual
(para 3. 10);
f) The Board will enter into arrangements with each SO and communities under such
terms and conditions as shall be acceptable to the Bank (para 3.23). The Board would
require SOs to keep separate accounts for each scheme and to adopt and follow the
accounting system prepared by the Board. Payment to an SO would be conditional
upon receipt of satisfactory updated scheme accounts on expenditures (para 3.39);



- 43 -
g) Support Organization selection would be carried out in accordance with eligibility
criteria agreed with the Bank. The Board would review the adequacy of the Support
Organization eligibility criteria at the end of the implementation phase of each batch of
schemes. Changes introduced would need to be acceptable to the Bank (para 3.25);
h) Scheme selection would be carried out in accordance with eligibility criteria agreed
with the Bank. The Board would review the adequacy of the Scheme eligibility
criteria at the end of the implementation phase of each batch of schemes. Changes
introduced would need to be acceptable to the Bank (para 3.27);
i) All individual contracts for goods and equipment for the Board and water supply and
sanitation schemes in excess of US$50,000 would be subject to Bank review and prior
approval. Contracts for consulting firms in excess of US$100,000 equivalent and
contracts for individual consultants in excess of US$50,000 equivalent would be
subject to the Bank's prior review and approval. All contracts in the first batch would
be reviewed by the Bank (para. 3.34),
j) The Borrower would open a special account. The Borrower would designate the
Board's Executive Director as its representative to operate the special account (para.
3 37),
k) Scheme accounts would be audited by an independent auditor recruited by the Board
after the second payment of the development phase and after completion of the
implementation phase. Signing of the implementation phase would be conditional
upon receipt of a satisfactory audit report, of the expenditures undertaken up to the
second payment under the development phase contract (para 3.39 and 3.40);
I) The Board would maintain records and accounts in accordance with sound accounting
practices to reflect the operations, resources, and all expenditures in respect of the
project. The Board's accounts, statements of expenditures and the special account
would be audited annually by the Kingdom of Nepal's Auditor General's office. The
Board would send to the Bank, no later than nine months after the close of its fiscal
year, a copy of its audited financial statements and audit report (para. 3.40);
m) The Ministry of Finance would make available on a grant basis such amounts as shall
be required by the Board for its operations (para. 3.41);
n) The Board shall submit its annual work program to the Bank two months before the
beginning of the fiscal year, and shall submit its quarterly and annual progress reports
to the Bank one month after the end of each quarter and two months after the end of
each fiscal year, respectively (para. 3.50);
o) A mid-term review would be carried out before June 30, 1999 (para. 3.52);

NEPAL
RURAL WATER SUPPLY AND SANITATION PROJEC'
Detailed Proiect DescinDtion
The project includes the following three components:
(a) The Rural Water Supply and Sanitation Fund Development Board
(b) Selection and Construction of Water Supply and Sanitation Schemes
(c) Institutional Development and Studies
A. The Rural Water Supply and Sanitation Fund Development Board (US$ 4.98 million)
2.    The Board was created, under the Development Board Act (1956), by a Formation Order
approved by a Cabinet decision dated ----------- -Following IDA review, the Board Rules were
approved by the Board on------------. The Board is an intermediary responsible to facilitate Rural
Water Supply and Sanitation service delivery through the private sector and with community
participation at all stages of the decision making process. The Board has no direct service delivery
responsibilities and will sub-contract these to SOs and communities themselves. Most training, most
monitoring and all auditing will be sub-contracted to Service Agencies (SAs). However, the Board
would have overall oversight over these activities. The Board would promote its activities through
public dissemination of information. This includes brochures, radio, newspaper, print media and visits.
3.    The Board would directly carry out the following functions: i) setting up eligibility criteria for
both SOs and Schemes; ii) identification, selection and contracting of SOs and Schemes; iii) selecting
and recruiting SAs to carry out certain functions (i.e. training, monitoring, audit); iv) day to day
management and monitoring of its portfolio (including all contracting and related disbursements); v)
preparation and implementation of its annual work programs and budgets; vi) overall management of
its operations (including its personnel, long term and short term consultants); and vi) selecting
consulting firms to carry out selected studies. This component provides funding for Board office
equipment, SO, SA and community training, Board staff training, Monitoring and Pre-Development
costs and Board recurrent costs.
Board Goods & Equipments (US$ 0.34 million).
4.    Board goods & equipment includes the provision of three vehicles, 8 motorcycles, a network of
office computers, all office furniture and miscellaneous office equipment.
SO, SA and Community Training (US$ 0.70 million).
5.    Training for SOs includes training on pre-feasibility studies, Community Action Planning,
Development phase proposal preparation, staff screening, training for community technician (on latrine
construction, sanitation, and CAP) and community worker (on HSE and CAP), and technical training
for overseers on design estimate and supervision. Training for communities include WUC treasurer



- 46 -
Anmex I
Page 2 of 10
training, regional Board-SO-Community training, community technician training and Tripartite
implementation phase contract orientation. SA training includes Pre-feasibility site appraisal
orientation, and training on Monitoring and Evaluation.
Board Staff Training. Monitoring & Pre-Development Costs (US$ 2.44 million)
6.    Activities include all Board staff training, Board Technical Assistance and audits, development
and implementation phase monitoring, pre-development site appraisals, publicity and dissemination
costs, impact evaluation, training materials adaptation & Development, water quality testing, SO
selection field visits, and monitoring of WUG legal registration.
Recurrent Costs (US$ 1.50 million)
7.    Recurrent costs include all staff salaries and allowances, office operating expenses and repair
and maintenance costs.
B. Selection and Construction of Water Supply and Sanitation Schemes (US$ 15.23 million)
8.    The scheme cycle spans 36 months and includes the following phases: Pre Development (13
months), Development (10 months) and Implementation (13 months). Activities within each phase
may vary in response to the particular situation found in a given community (community capacity to
organize) and as a result the duration of each phase may vary from community to community. This
component provides funding for Community Development as well as hardware activities within each of
the three above phases.
Community Development Activities (US$ 3.88 million)
9.    The purpose of the Community Development activities is to ensure people's participation in
decision making, to increase local ownership and effective use and sustainability of schemes, and to
build capacity to engage in other community development efforts. Community Development activities
will include three main activities; (a) Capacity Building Activities; (b) Hygiene and Sanitation
Education; and (c) Technical Support Services to Women.
Capacity Building Activities (US$ 2.13 million)
10.   Lessons from previous projects and the pilot project have emphasized that it is essential to have
a well organized community that is capable of promoting and managing community inputs to water
supply and sanitation schemes. The project will support specific activities to promote active
participation of the community in all stages of the scheme cycle. Capacity building activities will
include; (a) community organization/mobilization; and (b) complementary non-formal education.
(a)    Community Organization/Mobilization (US$ 1.88 million!. This includes a series of
orientation and training activities designed to enhance community capacity such as
group formation and leadership training leading to the formation, during the
Development phase, of an effective water user group (WUG), a representative water



- 47 -
Annex I
Page 3 of 10
user committee (WUC), with at least two women, and specific user groups capable of
planning, implementing, operating and managing water and sanitation schemes. The
project would support a community health worker (CHW) and a technician as basic
community level field worker supervised by a Community Development supervisor
and an overseer. Specifically, the following activities would take place in each
community:
(i)   information campaigns and getting to know the community. The CHW with
inputs from the Community Development supervisor would assess felt needs of
different groups within the community, reconcile these through continuous
dialogue with each of the groups and anticipate any conflicts that may arise.
(ii)   group formation and structuring to form a water user committee, and
orientation and training of WUC members (Treasurer training, management
training, Community Maintenance Worker training and Community Worker
training) to be able to participate actively in all stages of the scheme cycle. The
Community Health Worker (CHW) would be responsible for creating an
environment which would allow communities to engage im a process that
enables them to organize into groups and build confidence. The CHW would
assist women in their efforts to attain self reliance and would provide
orientation and training to the community health promoter and WUC members.
The technician with inputs from the overseer would assist the community
during the preparation stage to select designs, participate during construction,
and prepare the community to operate, maintain and manage the water and
sanitation systems built under the project by providing training of the
Community Maintenance Worker.
(iii)  participatory community assessment and action planning which would utilize
interactive principles, methods and techniques, and other social process
methods (e.g., Self Esteem, Associative Strength, Responsibility, Action
Planning and Resourcefulness (SARAR), Participatory Rural Appraisal
(PRA)). The project will encourage SOs to develop a set of simple
interventions on orientation, training and other means which would integrate
the organizational, health and sanitation education objectives of the project.
11.   Project support per scheme would include the salaries of a community health worker (CHW),
Community Development supervisor and material and travel costs. A total of four core orientation and
training activities would be carried out by the SOs (orientation and training on pre-feasibility studies,
WUC selection, leadership training and community action planning) and it is expected that SO input
would be highest during the development phase. Responsibility for community organization and
strengthening would be equally shared with the WUC during implementation with the SO role
expected to be phased down following construction of works as the community assumes full
responsibility for its water supply and sanitation schemes and other complementary programs.



- 48 -
Annex I
Page 4 of 10
(b)   Non-Formal Education (US$ 0.25 million). Experience has shown that community
education has been a useful starting point in mobilizing women in communities with no
previous experience in cooperative action and characterized as having low level of
organizational capability. Participatory non-formal education focuses on providing
women the opportunity to discuss their problems in an informal setting and increased
their problem solving capacity. It has also been found to accelerate the process of
development in communities that participated. In this case, people became more
receptive to new ideas, easier to mobilize, self confident, and are willing to take more
new responsibilities. The project would support the following activities:
(i)    The SO would assess the need for NFE during the pre-development phase.
Mass and group meetings would be carried out to discuss the need, level, and
intensity of NFE in each community. The SO will determine the number of
NFE classes and, on the basis of the level of intensity, it will identify potential
NFE supervisors and tutor/facilitators available in the community to be
included in the proposal. The proposal should show that it can access
experienced facilitator/tutor through previous experience and or available
NFE/SAs,
(ii)   SOs with no previous experience in organizing NFE classes would develop
partnership arrangements with available NFE/SAs for management support
including access to materials, supervisor and facilitator training and monitoring
visits. Payments for SA support would be included in the development phase
contract. The Board would provide a list of NFE/SAs available in the areas
with proven tract record in the field of NFE.
(iii)   Literacy materials and primers that have been developed both by the
govemment and non govemmental organizations would be used. The leaming
process would focus on courses dealing with water, hygiene, environmental
sanitation and community participation. The project would support the cost of
materials and tutor/facilitator salaries.
(iv)   NFE classes targeted especially for women would be held at the rate of one
class per 25 participants and funding support would be provided during the
development phase. It is anticipated that 50% of communities would require
NFE.
Hvgiene and Sanitation Education (HSE US$ 1.55 million)
12.   Many organizations involved in water supply and sanitation use different approaches for HSE
with varying degrees of intensity. HSE programs that use a participatory approach have been more
successful than those that rely on disseminating health messages. In addition, hygiene and sanitation
education activities that run independently of the water supply and sanitation projects are less effective
in achieving behavioral changes and improved hygiene conditions. The project would support HSE
that would be developed with the community, taking their knowledge as the starting point. Activities



- 49 -
Annex I
Page 5 of 10
would be integrated with water supply and sanitation activities from all phases of the project cycle.
Women will be the focal point of all HSE activities.
13.  The objectives of the HSE program are to: (i) inform and orient resident communities of water
and filth bome related diseases, causes, effects and prevention; (ii) implement a participatory HSE
program through trained community volunteers; (iii) orient and train SO/SA staff and govemment
health staff on participatory hygiene and sanitation education principles and methods; and (iv) build the
institutional capacity and capability of communities, SO/SAs, and local health centers as appropriate to
carry out hygiene and sanitation education activities more effectively.
14.  The HSE program would consist of: (i) promotional and media campaigns for hygiene and
sanitation related issues; (ii) orientation and training of health and volunteer workers in all
communities; (iii) equipping selected SOs with audio visual and other Community Development
matenals including specialized training to selected SO and health staff; (iv) training trainers to deliver
hygiene and sanitation messages to the communities; (v) support to hygiene and sanitation in the
schools covered by the communities involved in the project; and (vi) part-funding for latrines in project
communities through a community level sanitation lending fund..
15.  Approach and Strategy. It is expected that most if not all of the SOs would use a combination
of participatory and promotional approaches through the use of communication tools and techniques,
demonstration activities and cross visits where similar WSS schemes are being implemented. The
project would support the placement of female CHW through the SOs to bring about changes
particularly in personal and domestic hygiene. To promote the active participation of women, SO staff
would: (i) discuss with local leaders the need to involve women in water supply and sanitation services;
(ii) visit women individually particularly the poorest to solicit their participation; and (iii) organize
women into small groups to discuss issues and develop their problem solving capacity.
16.  The program would focus on improving personal, domestic, environmental sanitation and
primary health care. Emphasis would be given to bring about changes in those behavior that would
directly improve health. The baseline information on HSE will form part of the Community Action
Plan. Women would be involved in monitoring and evaluation activities by measuring changes in
hygiene practices such as water use and management, personal hygiene, domestic hygiene and
environmental sanitation. Since health improvements have been linked with other important factors
such as literacy levels and improved income, HSE activities would form linkages with non-formal
education classes, and support services to women activities.
17.   Target Groups for HSE would include leaders of different socio-economic, age, cultural,
religious and geographic categories such as community elders, traditional birth attendants (TBAs),
school age children with special emphasis to women's groups. The assumption made here is that
individual behavior depends on pressures to conform exerted by peers, and formal and informal
community leaders. Women as providers of family hygiene and sanitation play a crucial role in
affecting behavioral changes particularly their own young children. In addition, men usually control
finances and it is imperative that men also understand the factors affecting their health and that of their
families. School children, selected teachers and other government extension personnel are considered
important target groups and would be given priority.



- 50 -
Annex I
Page 6 of 10
18.   In each community, HSE activities would center on the following HSE activities and focus
groups:(a) Mother and Child Health focusing on health, hygiene awareness targeting the individual
household and women/tapstand groups; (b) Community and environmental sanitation, using
promotional and mass communication campaigns and demonstration activities,(c) Latrine promotion
program in support of the Sanitation Fund managed by the community, and(d) School program (where
possible) covering the school children and teachers.
19.   Methods and Tools. A combination of two methods would be used: (i) interpersonal and or
face to face communication, and (ii) mass media campaigns including the use of radio, wall posters,
leaflets, and other communication materials. HSE would be conducted in informal settings and by
using life situations and experiences. Community members would practice problem solving skills
through face to face discussion with the community/hygiene facilitator. It has been shown that HSE
activities are more effective in small group discussions usually 6-12 people. Hence small group
discussions on real life situations would be opted to modify attitudes and behavior. Complementary
techniques already being used by a number of SOs, some of them developed by the pilot project,
would be encouraged such as role playing, flip charts, the use of media, radio talks, video and motion
pictures, folk media such as drama, songs and games, and other social marketing techniques specially
to promote sanitation facilities would be used to repeat and reinforce messages.
20.   HSE orientation and training for women groups would be a priority activity through
tapstand groupings. Women would be involved in monitoring and evaluation activities by measuring
changes in hygiene practices such as water use and management, tapstand hygiene, personal, domestic
and environmental sanitation. Tapstand leaders would be trained on household observation and record
keeping of user acceptance of the new facilities. In addition, primary health care training designed for
the school teachers, community leaders, traditional birth attendants, and health post staff would
incorporate learning with development activities such as improving the environment by identifying
mosquito breeding sites caused by improper drainage, use of sanitary facilities, treatment for diarrhea
and skin diseases, growth monitoring etc. The orientation and training activities for HSE would be
integrated with the implementation of the overall capacity building activities. HSE orientation and
training would begin during the development phase and peak dunng the implementation phase.
Women's Technical Support Services (WTSS) (US$ 0.20 million)
21.   It has been shown that women's involvement in planning and management of water supply
improves performance and enhances benefit. Despite reported successes, institutionalizing the
inclusion of women in project planning and implementation will not be easy. A technical support
service to women would be established to focus on five key areas including, (i) skill enhancement
training to help women develop additional skills required to realize the benefits from improved water
and sanitation services; (ii) improvement in the access of women in the formal credit systems; (iii)
promotion of women in the planning and implementation of water supply and sanitation schemes; (iv)
operation and maintenance and resource mobilization activities on water collection, collection of user
charges, monitoring and evaluation; and (v) gender training and information for SOs, SAs and
community leaders. Project support would be in the form of training, material development and
provision of materials for Community Development activities.



- 51 -
Annex I
Page 7 of 10
22.  Planning for WTSS would take place during the development phase as part of the community
planning exercise. SOs would assist potential participants to define a menu of activities that could be
supported as part of the CAP. The proposal should show that women groups have been formed,
activities are clearly indicated and the proposal is prepared by the women groups themselves. It is
anticipated that priority would come from participants involved in NFE courses and tapstand members.
The number of activities would be small at the beginning to take into account the limited absorptive
capacity of the women groups as well as the SO/SA staff and the availability of other successful
programs operating in the locality. SOs would ensure that WTSS groups are well aware about the
organizations, opportunities, inputs and services available within the locality. Project support for each
community submitting an acceptable proposal would not exceed NRs 10,000 or Nrs 200 per woman
participant whichever is lower
Water Supply and Sanitation Schemes (US$ 11.35 million)
23.    Activities are divided into three phases; Pre-Development phase, Development phase and
Implementation phase. The activities include prefeasibility studies, survey and design of schemes,
construction cost of water supply schemes, school/institutional and household latrines, and catchment
protection activities.
24.    Pre-Development Phase (US$ 0.11 million). This includes the cost of inputs from an engineer
and a social scientist to carry out a prefeasibility study for each scheme. As part of the Pre-
Development phase, source flow rates will be determined by the SO and confirmed by the SA The
SO cost will only be reimbursed for those schemes selected for the Development phase.
25.    Development Phase (US$ 1.30 million). This covers the cost of an overseer for survey and
design for gravity schemes in the hills and shallow tubewells, deep tubewells and dug wells in the
Terai. It also provides funding for the Sanitation revolving Fund (US$0.41 million) for household
latrines. Household latrine construction will be financed from a Sanitation revolving Fund created
during the development phase. The Water User Committee will provide loans to households. Latrine
technology will utilize the most appropriate technology for the area taking into consideration
environmental issues such as soil conditions, water table and distance to water sources. Table A 1-I
lists the number of communities and the population served in the development phase of each Batch.

27.    All water supply scheme construction and rehabilitation activities designed in the Development

Catchment Protection (US$ 0.13 million)
28.   Catchment protection includes environmental impact mitigation activities consisting of
community tree planting for catchment protection in gravity schemes. Catchment protection includes
community tree plantation on about 5 ha of land upstream of the source managed by the users. The
project would support the cost of saplings.
Institutional Latrines Construction (US$ 0.83 million)
29.    The project will support the construction of two institutional latrines per scheme in either
schools or health posts.
C. Institutional Development & Studies (US$ 1.04 million)
30.   There will be a need for applied research and development studies to improve information
available to policy makers in designing sector policies and to test improved methods and techniques in
Community Development as well as in construction that are cost-effective. The studies to be carried
out and which are not included in the Board's M&E system are:
a)    Identification of criteria for scheme selection and corresponding scheme cycle in the
Terai (US$ 0.08 million). With a predominantly alluvial soil formation, a high static
water table, and a socio-economically stratified dense population, the Terai exhibits a
radically different challenge for the Board as compared to the hilly areas. The study
will identify and evaluate the operational feasibility of targeting Board investments on
those groups and individual households with least capacity to pay for or otherwise gain
access to RWSS facilities supplied through existing private sector provisions. Based on
its findings it will recommend a scheme cycle for Terai schemes including shallow
tubewells, dugwells, and sanitation improvement.
b)    Detailed demand studies (US$ 0.16 million). The study aims at developing reliable and
cost-effective methodologies for estimating demand for water supply and sanitation
facilities, and the valuation placed on them by rural communities, in order to improve
current parameters and assumptions used in pre-feasibility and feasibility studies as
well as in monitoring and evaluation. The study shall also assess and improve measures
of demand for different types of schemes used for calibrating design standards and
demand formation required under the present Board operations.
(c)    Preparation of follow-on project (US$ 0.24 million). This package of studies will
consolidate and incorporate lessons learnt and experiences gained from the Board
operations as well as other community based approaches in Nepal or within the region
in order to formulate a possible follow-on project.



- 54 -
Annex I
Page 10 of 10
d)     Special studies (US$ 0.31 million). As a result of the monitoring and evaluation as well
as recurrent supervisory visits a number of issues will emerge that require follow up
and focal in-depth studies. The JAKPAS experience suggests inter alia the following
issues: (i) identification of incentives to promote and adopt low cost technology
options; (ii) water quality control at source as well as in-home; (iii) community
perception of current eligibility, selection, and management procedures, including
choice of service levels; (iv) lack of incentives to promote cost-efficiency in the scheme
cycle; (v) health impact assessment. For each such limited focal study Terms of
Reference will be developed as required by the Board Secretariat, with the assistance
of the regional unit of the UNDP/World Bank Rural Water Supply Group providing
professional advice and assistance if needed.
(e)    National Planning Commission (NPC) and Ministry of Housing and Physical Planning
(MHPP) sector assessment and monitoring (US$ 0.25 million). This will compn'se
recurrent monitoring and assessment of different programs within the rural water
supply and sanitation sector, dissemination of experiences and lessons learnt to relevant
actors, and provide a basis for further clarification of the government policies in
support of community based water supply and sanitation. Such assessments will also
assist the NPC and MHPP in making actual program effectiveness in allocating
budgets over time.

NEPAL
RURAL WATER SUPPLY AND SANITATION PROJECT
TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR SELECTED PROJECT FINANCED STUDIES
L Identification of Poverty Focused Selection Criteria in the Terai
Justification
The Terai is the most densely populated region in Nepal, with an extreme socio-
economic stratification ranging from asse-less migrant labour to absentee landlords. From a
technical point of view the Terai is less complicated than the hilly areas, having a high static
water-table accessible through such simple technologies as dugwells and shallow tubewells for
which there is a local implementation capacity. However, past experience shows that neither
externally supported Rural Water Supply & Sanitation interventions nor private sector
operations reach the weakest sections of the population that is consequently forced to rely on
the most unsanitary facilities or sources. Against this background a study is required to
provide a feasible approach to socio-economic targeting of Board investments.
Objectives: The main objectives of the study are to
(a)   identify socio-economically relevant and operationally feasible criteria for
Board investments to reach the poorest underserved population;
(b)    propose a methodology for identification, selection, and appraisal capable of
being implemented by the Board;
(c)   identify and assess appropriate local support structures for scheme
development and implementation;
(d)   finaGse a Terai-specific scheme cycle based on the above.
Methodology: The study will be carried out as a series of overlapping activities:
(a)   inventory and compilation of secondary information available regarding the
demographic and socio-economic characteristics of the Terai, including
information available with local government bodies,
(b)   compilation of the performance and experience of DWSS and NGO operations
in the Rural Water Supply & Sanitation sector in the Terai;
(c)   inventory of the legal rights and entitlement issues of the squatter population
and landless labourers with respect to RWSS facilities or similar capital



- 56 -                       Annex 2
Page 2 of 6
investments made individually or collectively by them or on their behalf by
extemal agents (including government);
(d)    a limited number of community level RRA/PRA studies with poorer
households to identify strategic direct or proxy indicators of poverty/ capacity
to pay, nature and pattern of access to RWSS facilities, and forms of
collective/cooperative resource management (including existing or potential
CBO arrangements). Although limited in number, these studies must range
over different agro-ecological contexts of the Terai;
(e)    undertake a diagnostic sample survey in order to test the validity, feasibility and
range of variation of the target-group indicators suggested by the RRA/PRA
studies;
(f)    based on the above, the study shall compile a report comprising:
(i)    options of targeting Board investments, along with an assessment of
the pros and cons of each;
(ii)    a methodological guideline of scheme selection in the Terai, including
selection of support organisations, for adoption by the Board
Secretariat;
(iii)  identification of such institutional issues that require attention by the
Board in implementing the Terai component, particularly as regards
contractual issue and issues of entitlement and ownership of assets
created with Board assistance.
Staffing
The study will be recurrently guided and coordinated by an international short term
expert having considerable analytical experience of target-group and demand driven Rural
Water Supply & Sanitation in South Asia. Core national expertise include a rural survey
specialist familiar with RRA/PRA techniques, a socio-economist, and a sanitation engineer.
II. Detailed Demand Studies
Justification
The Project would support the delivery of demand led, cost effective and sustainable
water supply services to rural communities. Project success would depend on effectively
responding to such demand. Essential underpinning of project justification is that there is
demand for facilities, and the value of facilities exceeds the cost. In addition, understanding
demand is essential for determining optimal design criteria (households per tapstand or per



-  7-                        Annex 2
Page 3 of 6
well, distance to sources, etc.) and for developing viable systems for mobilizing as many
resources as possible from beneficiaries while ensuring that they realize potential benefits of
schemes. Estimating demand is difficult to do reliably and often seems to involve high costs.
Proposals based on 156 prefeasibility studies received for thePilot Project's second tranche
indicate community willingness to contribute 1-10% of the capital cost of gravity scheme and
1-30% of capital cost of tubewell schemes in cash in addition to all local materials, porterage
and labor. These are preliminary indicators and may not reflect actual community willingness
to contribute since SO staff undertaking prefeasibility studies would not have had intensive
interaction with the community at large. Nonetheless, such stated willingness to pay would
likely be lower than true demand for the service. While it took some time for JAKPAS
conmmunities (first batch) to understand the purpose of community cash contribution towards
capital cost it successfully collected minimum 1% of capital cost in cash. The proposed study
would seek to provide elements to improve demand estimation. This study would complement
other research and development (R&D) studies especially, monitoring and evaluation and the
health impact study.
Objectives: The main objectives of the study are to:
(a)    develop robust reliable and cost effective methodologies for estimating demand
for water supply and sanitation facilities, and the valuation placed on them by
rural communities, which can be used in carrying out prefeasibility, feasibility,
and monitoring and evaluation work; and
(b)   improve measures of demand for different types of schemes which can be used
to calibrate design standards and demand information required under the
present project.
Methodology: Demand for purposes of this study will cover, at a minimum, the quantities of
water consumed by households and the costs of collecting, storing and using it. The analysis
will also need to take account of:
(a)   the money valuation of water (in terms of the value of time saved from
improved water supplies, or using other methodologies); and
(b)   the possible implications of health considerations for valuation, for example,
differences in valuation when communities are aware of health benefits. These
topics are expected to be important issues in impact monitoring activities and
the health impact study. The exact definition of scope for the demand study
needs to be done in the context of these other activities. The study will
involve:
(i)    Review literature on rural water supply and sanitation demand, and the
relationship between demand and design standards. This would include
a review of World Bank reports and documents including Time for a



- 58 -                  Annex 2
Page 4 of 6
Change, and other literature on willingness to pay together with studies
which have been carried out in Nepal (including the AIIPH 1992).
(ii)   Review reliability and cost effectiveness of procedures being followed
by JGFFT and SOS under NRWSSP on the basis of analysis of data,
interviews with staff, and limited field work.
(iii)   Based on the above information, plus additional field work as required
regarding performance of past demand assessment methods, make
recommendations on improved methodologies for Support
Organizations, Board staff, and M&E activities.
(iv)   Field test the most promising methodologies. The tests should include
carrying out all relevant surveys and analyses of results. It would make
recommendations on future demand estimation for (a) Support
Organizations, and (b) M&E activities. Results would also include
proposed changes in benefit measures used in applying scheme
eligibility criteria.
(v)    Carry out revised test using best methodology from earlier steps, and
define detailed recommendations including sampling size,
questionnaires, timing and frequency of surveys, and systems for data
analysis, detailed training materials for use by SOs, Board, or M&E
staff for fuiture application of the methodology.
Staffing
The study would require inputs of economists and survey specialist. Additionally the
study would require short term inputs of an international economist (with substantial
experience in undertaking this type of study) since it is a relatively new area where local
experience may be shortcoming. The study would be undertaken in two phases depending on
tasks to be accomplished.
m1. NPC & MHIPP Sector Monitorin2 Studies
Justification
A special provision is made for sector monitoring activities to be managed by the
National Planning Commission and the Ministry of Housing and physical Planning. Efforts
would be made to disseminate, promote and monitor criteria and procedures adopted for the
Project such that there is a clear unified policy to support the community based approach.
Policies would have provision for integration of water supply and sanitation with software
activities such as health and hygiene education, training and other complementary programs
based on community needs assessment. The study would monitor and evaluate SO



- 59 -                        Annex 2
Page 5 of 6
implementation performance and project impact. Specific studies would be carried out during
the entire Project period to generate information on successes and failures to provide
information to define future policies and investment decision.
Objectives: The main objective of the study would be to:
(a)    review and evaluate the performances of Rural Water Supply & Sanitation
activities undertaken by different agencies;
(b)    formulate coherent and unified national policies for the sector based on proper
documentation and analysis of success cases;
(c)    disseminate lessons learned from the Project to other institutions in the sector,
(d)   identify ways of successfully integrating the sector with other sectors involved
in rural development; and
(e)    prepare a national level M&E system for the sector.
Methodology: The study would focus on the following activities:
(a)    Review programs of different agencies involved in the sector with reference to
monitoring and evaluation.
(b)    Undertake evaluation of performance of different Rural Water Supply &
Sanitation programs in light of cost effectiveness, reliability, sustainability and
replicability.
(c)    Recommend a unified policy for sector development, sector coordination and
integration with other sectors in rural development.
Staffing
Core staff required for the study include rural water supply and sanitary engineer, an
economist, a social science specialist and field survey staff. The study would be undertaken
over the Project period.
IV. Suecial Studies
Justification
As a result of the monitoring and evaluation as well as of the recurrent supervisory visits
a number of issues will emerge that require follow up and focal in-depth studies. The JAKPAS
experience suggests inter alia the following issues:



- 60 -
Annex 2
Page 6 of 6
(a)   identification of incentives to promote and adopt low cost technology options;
(b)   field studies on measures to promote water quality from source to house;
(c)   study of community perceptions of current eligibility criteria as well as selection
and management procedures, incl. choice of service levels;
(d)   identification of incentives to promote cost-efficiency in the scheme cycle;
(e)    health impact assessments.
to provide specific feedback to the relevant stakeholder of the Board operations on
core issues identified during the course of implementation;
to provide a basis for strategic learning and development, including consolidated
thematic inputs to forward programming of Board operations.
The studies will in most cases require procurement of local consultancy inputs, and in
exceptional cases also of international expertise. A total of 5 mm of international and 25 mm
of local experts, along with supporting staff has been envisaged.
V. Preparation of a FoIIow-up Proiect
Justficatio-
A preparation package to develop further follow-up programs incorporating lessons
learnt and experience gained from the present community based approach would provide
valuable policy and program level inputs for future director towards more effective delivery of
Rural Water Supply & Sanitation Services. Areas of interest for follow-up programs in
community based water supply would include (but not limited to) refining and developing cost
effective training and communication packages, development of low cost appropriate
technologies, skill enhancing income generating software activities for women, development of
acdvities to integrate the sector with other sectors of rural development. The project would
define specific activities and programs as it gains experience.

Consolidated Text
Whereas it is expedient to make adequate arrangements to
insure the smooth and expeditious implementation of development
pl~ans and projects,
Now therefore, His Majesty has framed and promulgated this
law on the advice of the Council of Ministers.
1.   Short Title, Extent and Commencement
(1) This law may be called the Development Board Act, 1956.
(2) It shall come into force at once throughout the
Kingdom of Nepal.
2.   Definitions
Unless repugnant to the subject or context, in this
act,
(a) Board means the Board formed under Section 3.
(b)  Foreign A enc  includes any foreign nation or
foreign or international agency.



As=4
- 68 -                        Pap2 04
(c) Notified Order means an order published in the
Nepal Ralapatra.
3.   Power of His Majesty's Government to Form Boards
(1)  In case His Majesty's Government so considers proper
or necessary, it may, through a notified order, form
a Board for the implementation of any development plan
or project prescribed therein.
(2) The functions and duties of the Board shall be
prescribed in every notified order published under
Sub-Section (1), and provision may be made also for
other incidental matters.
(3) The Board shall consist of such members as may be
appointed by His Majesty's Government.   The Board
shall be known by the name prescribed in the order
relating to its formation.
4.   Boards to be Regarded as Corporate Body
Every Board formed under Section 3 shall be a corporate body
with perpetual succession, and have a single seal for its entire
functions. The Board shall be entitled to conduct transactions
in and possess movable and immovable property like an individual.
It may sue or be sued in its own name like an individual.
5.  Fund of the Board
(1) The fund of the Board formed under Section 3 shall
consist of the following:
(a) Amounts granted by His Majesty's Government or
any non-governmental individual, and
(b) Funds provided by any foreign agency through His
Majesty's Government.
(2) The amounts and fund of the Board shall be utilized
for implementing programs in matters prescribed in the
order promulgated for forming the Board.
(3) The Board shall function in accordance with the
programs and working procedure prescribed in the rules
framed by it and shall maintain accounts accordingly.
But in case the order issued for the formation of
the Board under Section 3 so provides, such rules
framed by the Board shall require the approval of His
Majesty's Government.



- 69 -
Aza 4
Par3of4
6.   Employees of the Board
(1) The Board may appoint employees according to need to
conduct its functions smoothly. But no person shall
be appointed in any manner except in accordance with
the principles and procedure approved' by the Public
Service Commission.
(2) It shall be lawful for the Board to accept for some
time the services of permanent officers of His
Majesty's Government or any foreign agency.   Such
officers, while discharging the functions of the
Board, shall remain under its administrative control.
But the conditions of their usual service shall be in
no way affected by virtue of such assignment.
7.   Power of His Majesty's Government to Promulgate Orders and
Rules
His Majesty's Government may promulgate orders and
rules from time to time in matters relating to the
functions of the Board. It shall be the duty of the Board
to comply with such orders or rules.
S.   Dissolution of the Board
(1)  In case  His  Majesty's  Government  feels  that  any
development plan or project being implemented by the
Board can be operated more smoothly by His Majesty's
Government itself, or that the work of the Board has
been completed, it may dissolve the Board through a
notified order.
(2) Consequent to the dissolution of the Board,
(a) All rights, assets and liabilities of the Board
shall accrue to His Majesty's Government.
(b) All contracts concluded with the Board shall be
regarded  as  having  been  concluded  with  His
Majesty's Government.
9.   Settlement of Dispute Through Arbitration
(1) In case the agreement concluded with the Board
provides that any dispute arising in respect to such
agreement or its implementation shall be referred to
an arbitrator for decision, such dispute shall be
decided upon by an arbitrator appointed under the
agreement, and no court shall be empowered to hear and
dispose of such disputes.
(2) The arbitrator shall have the power of consulting
witnesses and evidence, subpoenaing witnesses and
procuring documents in respect to disputes referred to
him for decision under Sub-Section (1).



- 70 -
Amux 4
Pap 4 of4
(3) The decision of the arbitrator shall be final and
binding on both parties.
But in case the aggrieved party submits an
application, the Appellate Court may nullify such
decision in the following circumstances:
(a) In case there is prima facie evidence that the
arbitrator was guilty of misconduct while making
the decision, or that he made a wrong decision
with obvious dishonesty, or acted arbitrarily.
(b) In case the decision of the arbitrator is
manifestly contrary to law.
(4) In case such decision is nullified by the Appellate
Court, the dispute shall be referred for decision to
another arbitrator appointed by the Chief Judge of
such Court.
(5) The decision of the arbitrator shall be executed in
the prescribed manner by the collection office or any
office  discharging  the  function  of   collection
prescribed by His Majesty's Government.
(6) His Majesty's Government may frame rules to implement
the objectives of this section.
10.  Laws which Are not be Applicable
In order to remove doubts, it is hereby made clear
that the laws relating to revenue administration, official
documents or audit of Muluki Sawal (Administrative
Regulation) or the Muluki Ain (Legal Code) shall not be
applicable in respect to the functions of the Board.

RURAL WATER SUPPLY AND SANITATION PROJECT
Rural Water Supply and Sanitation Fund Development Board Order 2052 B.S. (1995)
DRAFT
In exercise of the powers conferred by Section 18 of the Rural Water Supply and
Sanitation Fund Development Board (formation) Order, 2052 B.S. the Board hereby
adopts and promulgates the following rules:
PRELIMINARY
1.    SHORT NAME, TITLE AND ENFORCEMENT
(1)   These rules shall be called the "Rural Water Supply and Sanitation
Fund Development Board Rules, 2052 B.S."
(2)   These rules shall come into force immediately.
2.    DEFINITIONS
Unless and until the context otherwise requires:
a)    "Order" shall mean the Rural Water Supply and Sanitation Fund
Development Board (formation) Order, 2052 B.S.;
b)    "Board" means the Rural Water Supply and Sanitation Fund Development
Board created under Section 3 of the Order;
c)    "Secretariat" means the Secretariat of the Board;
d)    "Employee" means an employee of the Board;
e)    "Technical Advisor" means a foreign consultant appointed by the
Board with the approval of His Majesty's Government to act as a technical
advisor to the Board and its Staff;
f)    "Technical Appraisal Committee" means a committee constituted under
Section 4 of this Rule to assess and select Support Organizations and
Projects to recommend for Board approval; and
g)    "Service  Agency"  means  consulting  firms  constituted  under  the
prevalent law to provide the services relevant for the achievement of the
objectives defined in the Order.
PROJECT ACTIVITIES
3.    PROJECT ACTIVITIES
In order to fulfill the objectives as outlined in the Order, the Board may
provide financial, technical or any other support to an eligible Support
Organizaticn and/or User Group to implement an eligible Project.
The Project shall consist of three phases: Pre-development Phase, Development
Phase and Implementation Phase. Pre-development Phase shall have the objective
of identifying the Project that would meet eligibility criteria defined under
Section  10 of this  Rule.   Development phase  shall have the objective of
increasing community consciousness on the importance of rural water supply nd
sanitation services and preparing the community to plan, implement, manage and
own the scheme. Implementation Phase shall consist of implementation of the
Project by the User Group with the support of a Support Organization and
preparing the community to operate and maintain the scheme.



82 -
Annex 6
Page 2 of ILI
An eligible Project may contain the combination of following components and
activities that directly benefit the delivery of sustainable and cost effective
water supply and sanitation to the rural population of Nepal:
a)    community mobilization;
b)    non-formal education;
c)    health, hygiene and sanitation education;
d)    training for capacity building of Support Organization and Water
User Groups;
e)    protection of water sources;
f)    water supply through gravity system;
g)    water supply through deep or shallow tubewell;
h)    water supply through dug wells;
i)    rehabilitation and/or augmentation of existing water systems;
j)    construction of public and private latrines;
k)    skill-enhancing training programs;
1)    enhancement of'the capacity of women to participate and benefit from
the Project; and
m)    any other activity that directly benefit the delivery of sustainable
and cost effective water supply and sanitation to the rural
population of Nepal or enables the rural community to realize the
benefits of Project.
4.    CONSTITUTION OF TECHNICAL APPRAISAL COMMITTEE
(1)   The  Technical  Appraisal  Committee  shall  consist  of  following
members:
a)    Executive Director             Chairperson
b)    Executive Staffs               Members
c)    Technical Advisor              Member
Provided that  the Chairperson may invite  any other person  to
participate in the meetings of the Technical Appraisal Committee as
an observer.
(2)   The procedure governing Technical Appraisal Committee meeting shall
be as determined by the Technical Appraisal Committee itself.
5.    SELECTION OF SUPPORT ORGANIZATIONS
(1)   The   Technical   Appraisal   Committee   shall   identify   Support
Organizations that meet eligibility criteria under Section 6 for each
tranche.
(2)   The Executive Director shall forward to the Board the evaluation
report for support organizations and the names of Support Organizations
that meet eligibility criteria and its recommendation of the Support
Organizations, selected to undertake the works for the corresponding
tranche.



- 83 -
Annex 6
Page 3 of II
(3)   The Board shall review the Support Organizations recommended by the
Executive Director on the basis of the findings of Technical Appraisal
Committee and shall approve their selection if they are found to be
eligible under Section 6 below.
Provided that the disapproval of any Support Organization by the
Board shall be supported by reasons.
6.    ELIGIBILITY CRITERIA FOR SELECTION OF SUPPORT ORGANIZATIONS
(1)   The eligibility criteria for selection of Support Organization under
Section 6 shall be as follows:
a)    Support Organization must be a legally recognized body;
b)    Support Organization must have constitutional provision to
engage in rural water supply and sanitation or rural development
activities;
c)    Support Organization must have proven and successful track
record of at least two years experience in participatory community
development and/or rural water supply and sanitation activities;
d)    Support  Organization must have  its accounts  audited  and
certified up to date; and
e)    Support Organization must have staffing capacity to carry out
the proposed services or demonstrated ability to procure such
staffing capacity.
Provided that for item (c) and (e) each Support Organization
shall be evaluated by the Technical Appraisal Committee using
ranking system in the Operational Manual. Technical Appraisal
Committee will use this ranking system to recommend the number
of  Support  Organizations  it  needs  to  undertake   the
corresponding tranche of Projects.
(2)   Board shall review and approve Support Organization eligibility
criteria prior to the commencement of each tranche of the Projects.
Provided that the eligibility criteria of Support Organizations
shall not be changed once finalized for each tranche.
SELECTION. APPROVAL AND IMPLEMENTATION OF PROJECTS
7.    PREFEASIBILITY STUDY
(1)   Support Organizations selected under Section 5 for each tranche
shall conduct prefeasibility study at the Project sites.
(2)   The prefeasibility study conducted by Support Organizations under
Sub-Section (1) shall focus on the possibility of meeting eligibility
criteria of the Project as per Section 10.
8.    PERMISSION TO SUBMIT PROJECT PROPOSAL
The Technical Appraisal Committee shall review the prefeasibility study reports
and shall identify the projects that would be feasible and meet the eligibility
criteria under Section 10.   The Executive Director then shall  invite the
concerned Support Organizations to submit detail project proposal for such
feasible projects.



- 84 -
Annex 6
Pape 4 of I
9.    PROJRCT APPROVAL
(1)   The Project proposal submitted under Section 8 shall be appraised by
the Technical Appraisal Committee and shall be verified by site
appraisals.   The Technical Appraisal Committee shall prepare a list of
projects identified as eligible under Section 10. The Executive Director
then shall forward the appraisal report with the list of eligible projects
and the recommended schemes for Board approval.
(2)   The Board shall review the appraisal report and shall grant approval
to the Support Organization to implement such Projects if it finds them to
be eligible under Section 10.
Provided that disapproval of any Project proposal by the Board shall
be supported by reasons.
10.   ELIGIBILITY CRITERIA FOR SELECTION OF PROJECTS
(1)   Eligibility criteria and indicators for selection of Projects shall
be as follows and these criteria would be applied at the development and
the implementation phases in the Project cycle using quality of
information available at that phase.
(i)   Need and Economic Viability
a)    Benefit Cost Ratio of at least 1.5.,  which could be
approximated at predevelopment phase by ascertaining whether
the average time saving expected per household would be over
2 hours (gravity schemes and Deep Tube Wells), 3/4 hours
(Shallow Tube wells), 1 3/4 hours (Dugwells) and estimated per
capita scheme costs were below defined threshold (1600 for
Gravity, 1300 for Deep Tubewells, 230 for Shallow Tube Wells,
and 1000 for Dugwells; or
b)    average per capita water availabilitv is less than 15
liters/capita/day and per capita costs are within the
thresholds referred to in a) above; or
c)    a maioritv of the households are dependent upon heavily
polluted sources and a scheme could provide cleaner water
through  protection  of  existing  sources  (Hills)  or  cost
effective amelioration of existing sources in the Terai and
per capita costs are within the thresholds of a) above;
(ii) Technical Feasibility
Proposed source(s) undisputed, unpolluted and yield at least
45 l/c/d (on the design population); only if there is no other
solution and existing sources are heavily polluted or provide
under 15 l/c/d, 25 l/c/d would be accepted; scheme meets
engineering standards and service levels set forth in the
Fund's Technical Guidelines, including measures to mitigate
adverse environmental consequences; and
(iii) Sustainability and Willinqness to Pay
a)    Community contribution to capital coSt (a) all unskilled
labor, locally available materials, porterage and (b) minimum
cash contribution of 2.5% of cash hardware cost in the Hills,
and 20% in the Terai and (c) 100% of additional costs for
higher service levels than those provided under Fund
Guidelines:



- 85 -
Annex 6
Page5oflI
b)    Satisfactory operations and maintenance arrancaements,
including:   (a) establishment of operations and maintenance
mechanisms, engaging Village Maintenance Worker, systems for
collecting maintenance funds on a regular basis, and (b)
upfront contribution of the first year's maintenance cost
(estimated at 3* of total hardware cost in the Hills, and 4%
in the Terai; and
c)    Establishment and registration of a Water User Group,
comprising all beneficiary families, and appointment of a
Water User Committee (WUC), with representation from each
cluster, to manage the scheme. The Water User Committee shall
include at least two women members.
d)    Evidence of coverage of all beneficiaries who wish to
participate.
Provided that Community willingness for all the above
would be appraised before the signing of the Project
agreement for Development Phase, and actual cash
contribution would be deposited and Water User Group
registration would be completed before the signing of
Project agreement for Implementation Phase.
(2)   Board shall review the Project eligibility criteria prior to the
commencement of each tranche of the Projects.
Provided that the Project eligibility criteria shall not be changed
once finalized for each tranche.
11.   IMPLEMENTATION OF PROJECT
The Board shall enter into Project agreement with Support Organization and User
Group after the Board has granted approval of the Project under Section 9. The
Project agreement for development phase shall be signed by the Executive Director
(for the Board), and the Project agreement for implementation phase shall be
signed by the Executive Director (for the Board), Support Organization and the
User Group.   The agreement shall specify the contractual obligations of the
parties as defined in the Terms of Reference.

13.   SELECTION PROCEDURE OF EXECUTIVE DIRECTOR
(1)   The post for the Executive Director shall be advertised in English
as well as vernacular national daily newspaper for at least three
consecutive days. The advertisement coverage shall include qualifications
required, brief description of the job and the duration of the assignment.
(2)   Selection of the candidate from the applicants shall be made through
interviews. Provided that the three best candidates shall be short listed
for final selection.
(3)   The selected candidate shall provide evidence of approved leave of
absence for a contract period or approved resignation from the previous
employer before appointment by the Board.
(4)   The Executive Director shall be appointed for four years.
Provided that the first six months of his assignment shall be the
probation period. The confirmation of his appointment shall take
place on satisfactory completion of the probation period. On the
satisfactory performance of duty, he/she may be reappointed for two
more years.
(5)   The  appointment  of  the  Executive  Director  shall  be  made  on
contractual basis.
(6)   The Executive Director shall not be related to any Member of the
Board or any employee of the Board.
14.   APPOINTMENT OF EXECUTIVE AND PROFESSIONAL STAFF
(1)   The procedure for the selection of Executive and Professional Staff
shall be as follows:
-     The posts for the above Staffs shall be advertised in the English
and vernacular national dailies for at least three consecutive days.
-     The advertisement shall include the qualifications required, brief
description on the job and duration of the assignment.



- 87 -                                    of It
-     The Executive Director shall screen the applications and conduct
interview for recommending two best candidates for each post for
final selection and appointment by the Board.
(2)   The selected candidate shall provide evidence of approved leave of
absence for the contract period or approved resignation from the previous
employer before appointment by the Board.
(3)   The selected candidate shall be appointed for three years.
Provided that the first three months of his appointment shall be
probation  period.      His  appointment  shall  be  confirmed  on
satisfactory completion of the probation period. He/She shall be
eligible for reappointment.
(4)   The appointment of Executive and Professional staff shall be made on
contractual basis.
(5)   The minimum qualification for the Executive and Professional Staff
shall be as follows:
a)    s/he must be a citizen of Nepal;
b)    s/he must be at least of masters in the concerned subject;
c)    s/he must have at least five years experience in the concerned
field;
d)    s/he must not be convicted of any immoral crime including
corruption or mismanagement of fund;
e)    s/he must not be holder of any political party office; and
f)    s/he must not be related to any Member of the Board or any
other employee of the Board.
15.   APPOINTMENT OF SUPPORT STAFFS
(1)   The Support Staffs shall be appointed by the Executive Director.
(2)   The Executive Director shall consult with the Executive Staff for
the appointment of the Support Staff.
(3)   The procedure for the selection of Support Staff and the minimum
qualification shall be determined by the Executive Director.
(4)   The selected candidate shall provide evidence of approved leave of
absence for a contract period or approved resignation from the previous
employer before appointment by the Board.
(5)   The Support Staff shall be appointed for three years.
Provided that the f irst three months of his appointment shall be the
probation period. The confirmation of his appointment shall take
place on satisfactory completion of the probation period.   They
shall be eligible for reappointment.
(6)   The appointment of support staff shall be made on contractual basis.
(7)   The support staff shall not be related to any member of the Board or
any employee of the Board.

(a)   The job description of the Executive Staff  (including Executive
Director) shall be as provided in the schedule B.
