NEPAL
RURAL WATER SUPPLY AND SANITATION PROJECT
Environmental Criteria
1.    The project will finance the construction of 900 schemes. A major point of this project is
to improve the environmental health of the community by supporting sanitation education, health
training, training on latrine construction, and technical assistance and materials to construct water
supply schemes. Environmental improvements lead to improved social and economic
improvements, especially for women and children. Some of the main environmental health
improvements include:
(a)   Time and energy savings for women and children by bringing water closer to the
home.
(b)   Increased water supply for bathing, washing and cleaning.
(c)   Improvements in hygiene and sanitation.
(d)   Reduced bacterial contamination due to controlled disposal of human waste.
2.    There are potential negative environmental impacts associated with the delivery of water
and sanitation services. Some of these impacts have already become evident in the pilot phase of
this project. The potential negative impacts include:
(a)   Contamination of the spring source during spring development and construction.
(b)   Induced erosion caused by pipe laying, general construction activities,
deforestation and improper drainage.
(c)   Improper construction of latrines leading to non-sustainable sanitation and ground
water contamination, especially in the Terai.
(d)   Improper construction of spring intake structure leading to short lived water
supply and possible destruction of the source.
(e)   Potential for cross contamination due to improperly joined pipe, broken valves or
leaky tanks.
(f)    Source tapping may deny water to previous users of the source not connected to
the scheme.
(g)   Tapping entire streams and springs cause downstream stream beds to dry up.



- 128 -
Annex 12
Page 2 of 4
(h)   Construction activities may induce deforestation and encroach on undisturbed
habitats at the spring intake structure, quarry sites and pipe trenches.
3.    Though there are inherent environmental issues in any water and sanitation program, this
project aims to insure the damage is minimized through lessons learned in the pilot phase, the
overall project approach and on-going training and technical manual provisions. The pilot phase
identified the above causes of adverse environmental impacts and subsequently several mitigation
measures were applied.
4.    The overall project approach targets both community participation and improved technical
and social assistance from decentralized support organizations. The training and education
components empower the support organizations and communities to construct and maintain long
lived systems. The project includes training and technical manuals which inform the Support
Organizations and communities on how to avoid the negative environmental impacts associated
with a project of this nature. Environmental measures will be included in the Community Action
Plan prepared during project development. More detailed environmental impacts and mitigation
measures are listed in the various training and technical manuals. Subsequent training and
technical manuals will continue to improve due to the built in monitoring and evaluation
component. A summary of mitigation measures are listed below.
5.    Site Clearance and Earth Moving - Site clearance, including tree cutting, and earth
moving during site selection and construction of intake structures, reservoirs, and pipe laying can
leave the affected area prone to erosion. Steep slopes and monsoons amplify the impact.
(a)   In order to avoid the monsoon season, the project has timed construction in the
dry season.
(b)   Construction and tree cutting on steep slopes will be avoided.
(c)    Soils will be properly compacted after backfilling.
(d)   Alternative siting will be considered for system components to avoid
encroachment to wildlife and minimize deforestation.
(e)   The project already includes tree planting to protect intake structures from
pollution and erosion. This will be extended to steep slopes and other erosion
prone areas where earth moving and site clearing occurs.
(f)    Unstable slopes will be avoided for quarrying of construction materials because of
the potential for landslides and damage to vegetation along footpaths.
(h)   Excavated material will be graded.
6.    Improper Drainage - Erosion and water logging due to improper drainage of overflow and
washout from leakage in water supply system components may occur. Improper drainage design



- 129 -
Annex 12
Page 3 of 4
and maintenance undermine the construction components (concrete tanks and pipes) by a)
increasing erosion which destabilizes the foundation and exposes pipes; and b) subjecting the
structures to weathering which decrease the structural integrity. Poor drainage from tapstands
and handpumps may create localized ponding of water. Ponded water is a breeding ground for
bacteria and disease carrying insects. Additionally, well water is susceptible to contamination
from surface water run-off.
(a)    Adequate drainage system around the structures and sanitary storm drains must be
maintained. The community health and sanitation education program as well as the
village maintenance worker training incorporates the need and the know how for
maintaining functional drainage systems.
(b)    Washing and livestock drinking facilities will be installed a safe distance down hill
from the tapstand to avoid cross contamination. The Community Action Plan will
assess the need for such facilities and incorporate then in the design.
(c)    Adequate drainage for overflow and washouts from water supply system
components should be provided.
(d)   Drainage from tapstands and handpumps must drain to a safe place such as near a
field, kitchen garden or soakaway.
(e)    New drainage routes will be avoided.
7.    Source Selection and Tapping - Source pollution due to improper design and site
selection poses a serious environmental health problem. Contamination may be caused by bacteria
or agricultural chemicals. Other consequences of source tapping include changes in natural
vegetation and water reduction for wildlife and cattle. The source may also supply water to
persons not in the project area thereby impacting their health and economic growth.
(a)    Procedures for testing water quality (such as simple presence/absence of bacteria)
will be investigated in conjunction with sanitary inspection at source
(b)    Proper design of intake structure to ensure protection from pollution and possibly
enhance treatment (i.e. sandfilter).
(c)    Sources must be non-disputed sources and downstream users must be protected.
(d)   Catchment area should be protected from deforestation and encourage tree
plantation to avoid future source depletions.
8.    Latrine Construction and Sanitation - Improperly designed latrines which do not consider
soil type, water table or drainage patterns, could pollute ground water and surface waters. Poor
soils for pit latrines include clay soils which do not drain and rocky soils which drain too quickly.
Since clay soils are impermeable and do not drain, pits built in clay soils can flood bringing



- 130 -
Annex 12
Page 4 of 4
sewage to the surface and essentially shortening the life of the latrine. The impact is two fold;
first, pathogens are brought to the surface exposing persons to diseases; second, persons who
traditionally defecate in the open fields will have negative connotations towards pit latrines and go
back to unsanitary habits. In quick draining soils, like rocky soils, the liquid waste drains too
quickly not allowing the bacteria in the soil to breakdown the pollutants. As a result, pollutants
make their way to the groundwater.
(a)    Siting of latrines and technology selection must consider soil type, depth of water
table, drainage patterns in the area (i.e. no nearby water sources downstream from
the latrines).
(b)    Training on latrine siting and technology will be carried out.
(c)    The project incorporates sanitation education to explain the link between human
waste and disease and explain the need for proper disposal.
9.    Design and Construction - The quality of design and construction of systems is
paramount in delivering long lived schemes. If the intake structure collapses after one year, the
intended environmental benefit is not sustained. The breakdown of systems such as leaky tanks,
broken or cut lines and broken handpumps, usually means loss of water for those at or
downstream from the failed component.
(a)   It is essential that the quality be held at the highest possible standards and that
standards improve with time.
(b)    The monitoring program will provide feedback on which systems work best, what
problems were found and why(i.e. poor concrete mixture, improper curing, poor
maintenance, or even lack of community involvement).
(c)    The project has developed a set of technical manuals and technical training to
improve design and construction quality. These manuals and training modules
must reflect lessons learned in previous construction years, based on the
monitoring program, and continue to improve.
(d)   Location of Livestock stalls shall be a minimum of 20 meters from any structure.
10.  Induced Development and Water Use - Improved access and increased supply of water may
settlement areas may also result from improved supply. The ultimate result is
increased demand from a limited source. Community education and planning must
consider these issues.



- 131 -
Annex 13
Page 1 of 11
NEPAL
RURAL WATER SUPPLY AND SANITATION PROJECT
JANTA KO KHANE PANI RA SAFAI KARYAKRAM (JAKPAS):
Summary of Performance and Lessons of Experience
I.     This annex focuses on issues most germane to the design of the institutional and
financial arrangements for the Nepal Rural Water Supply and Sanitation Project (the
Project). Detailed findings regarding specific components (e.g. content of training
courses, etc.) are reflected in the draft Board Implementation Manual drafted by JAKPAS
and to be finalized and adopted by the Board.
Summary
2.     JAKPAS set out to test a large number of questions in a very brief period. It has
succeeded in demonstrating that the basic project framework is sound. Communities are
eager to make major contributions to secure water and sanitation schemes which are
2
economically and technically sound. They contributed 40% of scheme costs up front.
There are growing numbers of SOs which are able to assist communities to plan and
implement water and sanitation schemes. It was feasible for a small number of highly
qualified staff in JAKPAS to administer a large number of schemes by using efficient
information systems, by making optimal use of local consultants to handle specific tasks
and peak period work demands, and by employing clear criteria for scheme selection and
payment by results. JAKPAS itself has adapted its procedures and operations as lessons
were learned. The Jakpas experience has shown that the duration of the original scheme
cycle (12-18 months) was too short and that the scheme cycle should be extended to at
least 36 months. Monitoring and evaluation of all aspects of Board, SO and scheme
performance is essential to ensure sound future programs. An effective future program
can and should retain the "learning by doing" approach pioneered by JAKPAS.
Background and Objectives
3.     The basic objective of JAKPAS was to set up and test a trial Rural Water Supply
and Sanitation Fund Development Board which would contract Support Organizations
(SOs), to assist communities to plan, implement, operate and maintain viable rural water
and sanitation schemes in a participatory fashion. Studies in 1991-2 with UNDP and
The objectives, work plan, status and fmdings to date of JAKPAS are set out in the nine Ouastley Progress Res orts coveing the peiod
fron project inoeption (March 12, 1993) to June 30, 1996. In additiu JAKPAS has issued a large number of reports on diffaert aspwts of its
operations, together with the draft Inplemer.tation mamlal for the Board These are contained m the project file (Annex 20). The pirnes review of
Jakpas Perfonmance and lessons leamned is based upon matenial contained in those reports and data in draft Project Conmpleion Reports for 29 frg
Batch projocts carried out whih 12 Support Organizations and draft SO accuznt reviewsr canied out for 28 SOs prepared in August-Septmnber 1995.
2
Scheme costs include: i) all labor (both skilled and unskilled); ii) all materials (both locally available and non locally
available); and iii) all transportation costs



- 132 -
Annex 13
Page 2 of 11
Japanese Grant Facility (JGF) funding, had established a prima facie case for developing
such an institution and the outline of a project approach.
4.    The central problem was that many previous supply-driven programs had failed to
provide sustained and sustainable water supply and sanitation services in the rural areas
(indeed, the initial approach by HMGN to IDA had been for a major project to rehabilitate
existing schemes, several of which had never delivered services.) The challenge was
therefore to design a delivery mechanism which would: (a) ensure full partnership of
beneficiary communities in all aspects of service delivery from project initiation to
operations and maintenance. This was required to ensure sustained improvements in
water supply and sanitation and consequent improvements in health and income; (b) make
effective use of the private sector, including non-government sector, to assist
communities, thus minimizing the drain on official budgets and administrative capacity,
and (c) identify large numbers of small dispersed and viable schemes without excessive
overhead cost.
5.    The following major JAKPAS sub-objectives were set out in the grant
application(s):
(a)   test and refine a variety of service delivery options (institutional and
socio-technical approaches) to be taken at local level, including approaches
which link women's organization, education and income with sustainable
water and sanitation service delivery;
(b)   refine the eligibility criteria for identification and selection of support
agencies and selection criteria for schemes;
(c)   based on the field testing of options, help define the funding mechanism
(financial and technical intermediary or Board) that will manage the Project
resources, including its policy and operations manuals and organizational
structure; and
(d)   under the two Japanese Grant Facilities (JGF) to prepare a pipeline of 125
schemes for implementation in the first year of the Project.
6.    JAKPAS started operations in March 1993 with funding from two Japanese
Facility grants, totaling approximately US$ 3.2 million equivalent. The first grant was
initially programmed to close December, 1994 (subsequently extended to April, 1995) and
the second planned to close on September 30, 1995 (extended to June, 1996).
Schemes Processed and Overall Performance
7.    JAKPAS has processed two "tranches" (hereafter "Batches") of schemes, and
identified a third Batch of around 102 schemes for consideration by the Board, as
summarized in Table 13.1.



- 133 -
Annex 13
Page 3 of 11
8.    About 101 SOs applied for prequalification under Batches One and Two, of which
56 met the pre-qualification criteria. These criteria comprised legal registration, a proven
track record (2 years experience) in rural development, capacity to engage appropriate
staff and satisfactory audited accounts. Twenty-four SOs were invited to submit schemes
for financing. The list was reduced from 56 to 24 in the light of the number of schemes
required for testing, and to test relative capacities of different types of SOs.
Table 13.1: Summary of JAKPAS Schemes, by Phase of Project Cycle
Batch      No. of Support "Ouota" of  Development Implementation
Organizations  Potential      Phase       Phase
Schemes*    Contracts  Contracts
One              14            na           86         74
Two              21            na           63         39(49)**
Three            25****       138         (102)***     na
l *    Number of schemes which pre-qualified SOs were invited to identify and prepare pre-
feasibility studies
* *   49 schemes were judged suitable for implementation (leaving 14 which were either non-
viable or required substantial additional work), but there was budget for only 39. It is expected
the remaining 10 will be supported by the Board during its first year program, in addition to the
102 Schemes Batch 3.
* * * Recommended by JAKPAS. The Board will need to decide whether to invite SOs to
prepare proposals for development contracts. Five additional projects may be added.
* * ** Of which 24 different SOs were involved in Batches One and Two and five new SOs
were prequalified for Batch Three.
9.    For Batch Three, 226 SOs applied. By that time, experience had indicated the
need for more thorough review of SOs' past experience (which JAKPAS verified by field
visits to past schemes). About 60 SOs were judged potentially capable of preparing
schemes. The list was reduced to the strongest 25 SOs in order to secure the economies
from having each SO handle several schemes while not exceeding the target to prepare
about 100 schemes for Batch 3.
10.   As is apparent from the above table, there has been substantial attrition in scheme
numbers from one phase to another. While there have been a variety of reasons for this,
including failures by SOs and/or communities to mobilize sufficiently, over 80% of
"drops" have been due to water source issues. In most cases, the problems were
technical: inadequate flow and inability to find satisfactory alternative sources, or
inappropriate siting (e.g., source below population to be served). Disputes within the
community or with neighboring communities regarding the use of the source and lack of
alternatives has also been a factor in some drops, although the more normal situation is for



- 134 -
Annex 13
Page 4 of 11
source disputes to be resolved with some adjustment in numbers of households or service
levels. The prime lesson from this experience has been to require SOs to prepare more
thorough pre-feasibility studies and JAKPAS to carry out more rigorous site appraisals on
all schemes (rather than a sample). The pre-feasibility studies include source measurement
during the driest season, April/May, when flow rates are lowest)). This has resulted in
some increase in estimated project costs (for SO training, more site appraisal work) as
well as a lengthening of the scheme cycle. While attrition may be minimized by
appropriate measures, it cannot be eliminated, and so project costing allows for 13%
attrition between development and implementation.
I1.   As of June, 1996, construction is completed for 1 10 schemes and underway for 3
schemes for which training, health and sanitation and building community capacity for
operations and maintenance are still under way. In several instances schemes for which
construction was "complete" were not providing water due to poorly constructed intakes
or landslides. Corrective measures were taken and the problem corrected. In several
additional schemes, the quality of intakes and pipelines (trench depth, location, works at
river crossings) needed to be upgraded if the schemes were to achieve their expected 15 -
20 year service life. These problems have arisen partly from design defects, and partly
from poor construction quality. Both problems have been related to inadequate staff
inputs, in terms of quality and of time in the field during design and construction.
Corrective measures have been introduced for Batch 3. Evidence is not yet fully available
on how many completed schemes will be failures as regards their construction
components. However, all 1 10 schemes are currently delivering water as intended.
12.   Experience with the Community Development components has been mixed. The
initial project completion reports for Batch I schemes indicate doubt about the viability of
operations and maintenance arrangements (appointment of Village Maintenance Worker,
regular collection from households to pay his/her expenses, regular maintenance as
contrasted with repair of systems) in up to one half of Batch One schemes. While many
schemes exhibit impressive latrine construction, quality is deficient. While ex-post
Knowledge, Attitudes and Practices (KAP) studies (carried out in 9 schemes) found
significant improvements in some health related behaviors (hand washing, bathing, latrine
use) in some communities, there was little improvement in others, and even the "best"
communities had much room for improvement. Responses to these problems imply
somewhat longer development and implementation phases, better definition of community
Development inputs, and more rigorous monitoring (discussed below). Appropriate
corrective measures have been introduced for Batch 3 schemes.
Hills/Terai
13.   Of the total 113 schemes for which implementation contracts have been signed, 95
are in the hills and 18 in the Terai. The reasons for the relatively low "demand" from the
Terai (compared with population, or official "coverage" figures) probably include: (a)
Terai schemes may be less attractive to SOs than gravity schemes, (b) fewer communities



- 135 -
Annex 13
Page 5 of 11
meet the eligibility criteria regarding demand and need. This could be due either to: (i)
few communities with high need, or (ii) excessive JAKPAS time savings as would occur if
initial cost were overestimated. JAKPAS criteria were based on recovering costs of the
relatively conventional "package" of new handpumps or dugwells. Some evidence
indicates that assistance with upgrading existing facilities and using lower-cost options
would prove more attractive, (c) failure by SOs to identify and develop schemes for the
most disadvantaged communities where the eligibility criteria would be met, (d) a larger
"communications gap" between JAKPAS (in Kathmandu) and communities and SOs in the
Terai than in the Hills, implying the need for special efforts to elicit interest from Terai
SOs. An early study is proposed to explore this issue, and to recommend improved
mechanisms for the Terai component.
Cycle Timing
14.   The three project phases were initially expected to be, respectively, 2-3 months, 4-
6 months, and 6-9 months, for a total scheme cycle of 12-18 months. No scheme has
achieved these targets. Some delays have resulted from a mismatch between the timing of
the grants and the seasons of Nepal. But more fundamentally, the calendar of seasons,
harvests and holidays, with their influence on accessibility of locations, availability of local
labor, and ability to construct, plus time needed to process proposals, imply a longer cycle
than assumed in earlier preparation studies. For gravity schemes, experience has
demonstrated that successful project completion must meet three climate-related
constraints: (a) measurement of source adequacy in the dry season (April/June), (b) start
of construction following harvest (November, say), and (c) completion of all construction
activities, including correction of faults, before the monsoon (say mid-June). These
constraints, plus time required to do the work of each phase, implies a scheme cycle on the
order of about 36 months of which about 23 would be under contract (compared with the
initial estimate of 10-15 months). For this reason a scheme cycle of 36 months has
been recommended for the Project. Length for Terai schemes requires additional study.
Eligibility Criteria
15.   Securing reliable information for decisions is perhaps the major single challenge
faced by any decentralized program. Earlier preparation work recommended defining
clear criteria for scheme selection if resources were to flow to communities in need, if
projects were indeed to improve the situation, and if program overhead costs were to be
minimnized. JAKPAS has tested the criteria defined for testing, and introduced several
improvements. The three most important lessons learned were that (a) the initially-
proposed criteria were fairly appropriate, (b) criteria need to be continuously reviewed and
updated in the light of experience from monitoring and evaluation, and (c) criteria in effect
for any particular Batch must be clearly stated and rigidly applied to minimize
uncertainties among SOs and communities. Specific lessons are noted under the three
main criteria: demand/economic viability, technical feasibility and sustainability.



- 136 -
Annex 13
Page 6 of l1
16.   Felt need/Economic Viability: This criterion was considered to be met if estimated
daily time saving per household were large enough to yield a benefit/ cost ratio of 1.5 for
estimated average scheme construction costs. (A ratio of 1.5 was to allow for risk, and
costs of Community Development and program overheads). In the hills, this implied a
time saving of 2 hours per household per day, and in the Terai one half hour (Shallow
Tube Wells). For schemes with average cost above a defined threshold, they should show
a B/C ratio of 1. 5, or initial consumption under 15 I/c/d or more than 50% of households
depending exclusively on heavily polluted sources. Experience has yielded three major
lessons. First, SOs remain largely unconvinced of the importance of the "need" criteria.
Hence, JAKPAS has had to increase the rigor with which it verifies need indicators during
site appraisals. Second, it has proven infeasible to reject a scheme following the
development phase on the basis of "need/economic viability" criteria alone. The extent
and quality of the pre-feasibility study and pre-development phase site-appraisal have
therefore had to be augmented to permit a more reliable judgement on need and economic
viability. Thirdly, some First Batch schemes may have had inadequate time-saving (there
was only sample site appraisal, and then rather cursory). But Batch Two and Batch Three
experience indicates no shortage of communities which would potentially benefit from
economically viable schemes. Even if the thresholds must be raised (to reflect expected
increased scheme costs), there is no doubt that demand will exceed capacity to meet it for
the foreseeable future.
17.   Technical Feasibility: Technical feasibility criteria comprise (a) adequate source
yield and availability of an undisputed source (discussed above); (b) relatively unpolluted
source or availability of in-home treatment. In-home treatment is a subject proposed for
study during the project to determine if in-home treatment can work and, if so, how; (c)
adherence to stated service levels, with communities to pay the full cost of higher service
levels. This criterion is proving difficult to administer, as there are enormous pressures to
increase tapstands over the number designed; and (d) consistency with established Board
engineering standards. The main emerging issue concerns how best to incorporate the
remnants of other systems in scheme design. The engineering standards have been
developed largely by various donor supported projects to construct new schemes. This is
manifestly inappropriate to the Terai where there may be opportunities to upgrade existing
private technologies or facilities. Developing robust procedures to upgrade or make use
of existing facilities in the Hills and Terai will require a higher level of skill and different
orientation to that implied by past practice, and appropriate modification to design
manuals. Difficulties with landslides during the first two Batches also suggest the need for
more systematic review of possible landslide risk in judging viability of schemes or in
designs adopted. Construction practices and training need improvement, specifically: (a)
locating structures in appropriate areas (i.e. spring boxes to the side of the stream not
directly in the stream); (b) hydraulic designs need to ensure break pressure tanks, tap-
stands and reservoirs allow the water to flow evenly through the community; (c) drainage
around the structures need to flow away from the structure; (d) water, cement, sand and
aggregate ratios need better quality control to ensure strong cement/concrete mixtures;
and (e) sanitation technologies, including siting of latrines and understanding soil types,
need improvement.



- 137 -
Annex 13
Page 7 of 11
18.   Sustainability: The sustainability criterion consists of: (a) willingness of the
community to contribute all local materials, porterage and unskilled labor, a percentage of
construction cost (initially 1% for the Hills, but raised to about 2.5% in Batch 3, and 10%
for the Terai, subsequently raised to 20%), and to contribute to an O&M fund (3% of
gravity scheme and dugwell cost, 4% of shallow tubewell and deep tubewell); (b)
contribution of 100% of the incremental cost of implementing higher than standard service
levels; (c) registration of a water users group; and (d) development of satisfactory plans
for future O&M. Sustainability at pre-feasibility stage is assessed on the basis of
judgements about community willingness to participate (point (a)). Actual contributions,
plus evidence of fulfillment of the other indicators is required before signing
implementation phase contracts. Experience has completely dispelled earlier uncertainties
regarding community willingness to pay. Communities contributed about 38% and 40% in
the First and Second Batches respectively towards construction (materials and equipment,
skilled and unskilled labor and transportation) costs. They raised additional amounts for
Operations and Maintenance. Defining appropriate user groups in the Terai still requires
attention (since co-operation on a wider scale than the users of a specific well or pump is
difficult to organize).
Service Delivery Options and Lessons
19.   It was initially expected that individual SOs would implement different packages
(Non-formal education, different approaches to health and sanitation education,
stimulating womens' activities, etc.). An evaluation of differing programs would enable
adoption of successful options through a process of cross fertilization and emulation.
JAKPAS also developed a series of tools designed to stimulate community action planning
and carried out training of SO staff in their use so as to foster full community participation
in all phases, and hence "ownership" and sustainability of schemes. The application of
these tools was left to the discretion of the SOs. Some JAKPAS community action
planning tools have subsequently been used successfully by NGOs in other sectors. There
have not been enough ex-post evaluations of scheme performance to indicate the relative
usefulness of different specific interventions in producing sustainable schemes or improved
health practices. Moreover, judgements on the efficacy of different packages is hindered
by uncertainties about just what packages have been implemented in some cases (as
discussed further below). In response to these difficulties, JAKPAS has tended to
standardize the packages, and types and levels of inputs to be financed (health training,
women's support services, option for Non-Formal Education during development phase
only, etc.). Despite such standardization, there will undoubtedly be differences in program
emphases and impacts with implications for future scheme design.
Performance of Communities. SOs and JAKPAS
20.   The weaknesses--and successes-- in outcomes mentioned earlier arise as much
from the nature of the inter-actions between communities, SOs, and JAKPAS as the



- 138 -
Annex 13
Page 8 of 11
actions of any one of these parties. For descriptive purposes it is convenient to describe
the problems in terms of each actor, however.
Community Performance
21.   JAKPAS has demonstrated that the vast majority of communities are prepared to
contribute substantial time and cash to secure sustainable water supplies. Many
communities have also demonstrated a willingness to make social changes (e.g. enable
women to take decision-making roles beside men in managing water schemes) to improve
water services. It was known from the start that communities would need assistance to
overcome basic ignorance (e.g. on technical matters and health practices) and lack of
traditions for co-operation in creation and maintenance of common facilities. To the extent
that there is room for improvements in community performance, experience to date
indicates that they must be sought through more effective measures by SOs and JAKPAS
to build capacity and seek balance with communities.
SO Performance and Lessons
22.   There was major initial uncertainty as to whether SOs would be prepared to co-
operate with JAKPAS in a fairly highly structured environment of eligibility criteria, tight
implementation periods, and payment according to performance. In these circumstances,
the strategy of Batch One was to give maximum autonomy to SOs to accomplish results
(from which lessons could be drawn). JAKPAS took measures to correct specific
problems which arose, especially in supplementing weak technical capacity of some SOs.
These measures were non-replicable for the future, but were useful during the learning
phase.
23.   JAKPAS selected a variety of SO types to test their relative competencies. They
included an International NGO (INGO) which supervised community based organizations
(CBOs) which would not have met SO eligibility criteria on their own) national level
NGOs which have operations in several districts, district level (and regional level) NGOs
with operations in single districts or contiguous districts, Local NGOs typically working in
part of a district and private firms which had undertaken development work.
24.   While there were substantial differences in performance among SOs, they were not
systematically associated with any SO type. The expectation that local NGOs would have
fuller communications with communities and stimulate wider local participation was borne
out only in part. The demonstrated past ability of the national NGOs to engage competent
staff was not reflected in superior field performance under JAKPAS. The following
weaknesses were sufficiently widespread in Batch One to require systematic remedies in
Batch Two and in the future:
(a)   Weak community management, as exhibited by failure to carry out
community action planning meaningfully, a tendency of some SOs to deal
with small groups in the community or even to designate water users'



- 139 -
Annex 13
Page 9 of 1I
committees, failure to plan activities jointly with the community,
insufficient involvement of community in design of schemes and in handling
funds and procuring goods (including goods procured with cash
contributions from communities), and failure to train WUC committees.
(b)   Inadequate technical capacity of SO field staff (in Community
Development and Construction). This was in some cases due to
inadequate initial qualifications, but also failure by the SO to provide staff
with appropriate training. In some cases, SO staff trained by JAKPAS
were not assigned to the field, or failed to pass on the results of their
training to field staff. In other cases, skill gaps have been identified which
need to be filled. An important gap concerns lack of skills by design staff
to integrate the community into the design process. The requirement for
communities to contribute a percentage of project cost was partly to induce
more accountability by designers to community preferences. In many
cases, the designers themselves are unable to design and cost alternatives
which meet basic engineering standards, as contrasted with costing a single
system based on the design manual.
(c)   Insufficient field time by SO staff. This was a major factor in poor
construction executed by communities without technical supervision. This
failure was reflected in poor or non-existent records on staff disposition at
SO headquarters.
25.   The principal solutions to the above problems follow clearly from their description
and several have already been introduced in Batch Two. They include: more attention by
JAKPAS to managing its portfolio managers and to the qualifications of staff assigned by
SOs, training to fill gaps in experience, more detailed programming of work inputs and
outputs to provide clearer performance monitoring indicators, more detailed contractual
performance requirements for receiving payments, better SO record keeping and audit of
accounts on previous contracts before signing new ones.
26.   To increase SO accountability to communities, communities are now being
requested to fill time sheets for SO staff inputs before contract payments. It has also been
decided that SOs would establish joint accounts with Water User Committees for
construction costs during implementation phase so as to stimulate more transparency and
ensure cooperation in implementation.
27.   The various weaknesses listed above are not universal. JAKPAS has
demonstrated the existence of many SOs able to fulfill scheme responsibilities. Moreover,
through its operations, JAKPAS has substantially increased capacity of several SOs to
participate in future schemes. It is likely that as tighter compliance monitoring is
accepted, it will be possible for JAKPAS to increase its attention to monitoring the
effectiveness of different packages.



- 140 -
Annex 13
Page 10 of 11
JAKPAS Performance
28.   JAKPAS accomplishments in terms of completed schemes, refined criteria,
management tools and establishing a viable organization during a very brief period are
impressive. Even if these accomplishments could not have been bettered in the past,
however, several lessons for future improvement have been incorporated into Project
design.
29.   JAKPAS Organization: JAKPAS has adopted an operational organization and
staffing which should be suitable for the Board. It is managed by the Chief Technical
Advisor who reports to the World Bank which is responsible for administering the
Japanese Grant Facility. This is the principal difference between JAKPAS and the Board,
since the Executive Director of the Board will report to the Board. JAKPAS has
established an Advisory Committee of HMGN and private sector members with similar
qualifications to those expected for the Board, but with advisory rather than decision
authority.
30.   The Chief Technical Advisor (CTA) is assisted by the National Advisor who acts
as his deputy. The detailed organization has evolved from 1993 to 1996, and its latest
form is proposed for the Board.
31.   Initially, all operations staff (including the CTA) had responsibilities as portfolio
managers responsible for overseeing all aspects of contracts with individual SOs and
communities together with functional responsibilities. This "matrix" pattern proved
difficult to sustain as the scale of JAKPAS operations expanded. Three Portfolio
Managers retain some functional responsibilities for JAKPAS-wide operations (Gender,
Monitoring and Evaluation (M&E), and Health and Sanitation Education (HSE)).
32.   Unit Heads and the relevant Portfolio Manager participate in the Staff Technical
Appraisal Committee (STAC) which meets under the National Advisor and reviews
pre-qualifications of SOs as well as suitability of each proposed scheme and recommends
acceptance or rejection to the Chief Technical Advisor.
33.   Adequate field supervision of SOs and schemes requires use of substantial numbers
of professional staff which vary over the project cycle. Numbers have ranged from 5 (at
the commencement of JAKPAS) to 58 (during the peak activities in mid-1995). As of end
September, 1995, 44 professionals were engaged either in central management functions
or some aspect of scheme supervision, monitoring and evaluation.
34.   JAKPAS has demonstrated that it is possible to engage staff with the qualifications
and experience required to manage a program of this type. Heavy reliance was placed
initially on engaging regular staff at United Nations rates (as required under IDA-
administered programs), and the number of staff on these terms peaked at 20 in September
1994. Subsequently, in order to avoid bureaucratic bloat, to tailor staffing to changing
needs, to promote increased accountability for performance, and to minimize costs while



- 141 -
Annex 13
Page II of II
ensuring quality of manpower, more reliance has been placed on professionals from SAs
contracted to supply professional inputs on an as-needed basis. As of end-September,
1995, there were 9 regular staff (including the CTA and National Advisor), and 35
temporary SA staff.
3 5.   JAKPAS has developed an efficient computerized Management Information
System. This is essential for managing a large number of contracts with minimal central
staff This system should continue to evolve. There remains scope for additional training
of all professional staff to take full advantage of the system. JAKPAS has also issued a
series of technical guidelines and manuals which set out in detail all the procedures
(including rationales, forms, actions, draft contracts, drawings, etc.) required for Board
operations (Annex 20).
36.   Portfolio Management: As noted above, JAKPAS has defined more clearly the
content of SO contracts to enable more effective performance monitoring. In addition,
there is a need to enhance multi-disciplinary competence by monitoring staff to ensure
maximum effectiveness in advising SOs and communities in the field (e.g., "social" staff
have had to learn more about technical aspects of schemes, and vice versa). Timeliness of
JAKPAS field monitoring has also been a problem. This has been addressed through
engagement of SAs, including SAs based in regional centers. Lack of continuity in
supervision staff to particular SOs and schemes has also caused some confusion but has
been reduced through better planning of portfolio management activities.
37.   While Communities, SOs, and JAKPAS are joint signatories to implementation
phase contracts, the JAKPAS relationship with communities has been largely through
intermediary SOs. Administrative feasibility precludes JAKPAS dealing individually on all
matters with each community. Nevertheless, there is a case for seeking a better balance in
the JAKPAS-community relationship. Various methods are being tested, including having
communities approve SO time sheets, placing more emphasis on tri-partite contract
signing ceremonies, and requiring more systematic contact with communities by JAKPAS
monitoring personnel.
38.   Monitoring and Evaluation: The main emphasis has been on performance
monitoring of contracts by portfolio staff. The initial set of Project Completion Reports
were prepared by independent SAs. These, together with the reports on SO accounts
undertaken by an independent accounting firm, have proven invaluable in reviewing
overall performance, and identifying issues requiring attention. The usefulness of these
reports fully justifies the proposal for independent technical and financial audits. Impact
monitoring has so far received relatively little attention compared with implementation
monitoring, in part because so few schemes have been completed. KAP studies of 9
communities (base-line and ex-post) have provided some tantalizing data. There is a need
to enhance the attention given to impact monitoring in completion reports (this was
unaccountably excluded from TORs for the first project completion reports). The Board
will clearly benefit from devoting adequate resources to monitoring and evaluation and
periodic special studies on various aspects.






- 143 -
Annex 14
Page 1 of 6
NEPAL
RURAL WATER SUPPLY AND SANITATION PROJECT
Support Organizations and Service Agencies
I.    The institutional partners of the Rural Water Supply and Sanitation  Fund
Development Board are classified into 2 broad functional categories:   Support
Organizations (SO), and Service Agencies (SA). These include NGOs, community based
organizations, and private groups. A comparative assessment of institutions available for
rural water supply and sanitation service delivery was made based on a review of a sample
of organizations engaged in RWSS activities and experience from the JAKPAS pilot
project.
A. Support Organizations
2.     Support Organizations (SOs) are organizations that would assist participating
communities to implement their water supply and sanitation schemes. They include non-
gyvernmental organizations, commnunity based organizations, and private sector firms. The
role of the SO would be to facilitate and enable communities to take a leading role in the
decision making process concerning the identification, design, implementation and
operation and maintenance of their Rural Water Supply and Sanitation schemes.
Non governmental Organizations
3     Non-governmental organizations (NGOs) are classified into 5 categories based on
ability to undertake activities in one or several districts. These include national, central,
district, local, and international NGOs. Non-governmental organizations are typically
managed by a volunteer executive Board consisting of 7-11 members each with a
designated position. Board members are responsible for negotiating with funding agencies
and supervising implementation. Many NGO members are socio-politically motivated and
enjoy considerable influence within their own community. There is also a tendency for
NGO leaders to develop patron-client relationships with their beneficiaries. The more
closed an area the more intense these relationships appear to be. NGOs whose Board
members consist of professionals are more development oriented, employ skilled personnel
and rely less on voluntary work. Between 12,000-15,000 NGOs are reported to operate in
Nepal. Of the 6,000 registered under the Society Registration Act, about 3,400 are
registered under the Social Welfare Act (SWC).
4     National NGOs. About 18 national organizations have chapters in most if not all
75 districts. They generally have a well institutionalized management structure and are
often bureaucratic. As a result projects are often target oriented. They provide services on a
national scale through their district based branches by mobilizing voluntary workers and a



- 144 -
Annex 14
Page 2 of 6
few paid staff. Elected branch executive members manage professional and voluntary
workers who are recruited to carry out its activities. The Nepal Red Cross Society (NRCS)
with chapters in all 75 districts is the leading NGO in the water sector with 10 years of
experience. Many chapters of NRCS have considerable experience in constructing water
supplies. For instance, NRCS of Kavre district in the JAKPAS pilot project have
implemented 60 water supply schemes. The Family Planning Association of Nepal (FPAN)
as well as Nepal Jaycees and Lions Club operate in 24 districts. Their absorptive capacity is
high as evidenced by their linkages with international networks. Their major strength is the
availability of sector specific expertise and a well established network.
5     Central NGOs. These are organizations that operate in more than one district from
the center (Kathmandu) through fielding of supervisory and field staff. To date, more than
75 are registered under the SWC. Organizations working under the Jakpas pilot project
include Support Activities for Poor Producers of Nepal (SAPPROS), Institute for Integrated
Development Studies (IIDS), and Forum for Environment and Development (FED).
Central NGOs are observed to implement projects on behalf of beneficiaries. In addition a
few conduct research and development studies while some are observed to act as facilitators
for the development of locally based organizations. A major advantage is their ability to
procure skilled professionals on demand, and to serve as a bridge between the Board and
grassroots organizations.
6     District NGOs.  District NGOs are organizations that operate in several Village
Development Committees (VDCs) of one or more districts such as Tamakosi Sewa Samiti
(TSS), Samaj Sewa Samooh (SSS) and Society of Local Volunteers Efforts (SOLVE) in the
pilot project. More than 500 organizations are registered in this category. They undertake a
wide range of activities in different sectors including water supplies, health and sanitation,
NFE, agroforestry, animal husbandry, environment, family planning and income generating
activities. District NGOs are more likely to increase the number of activities in a particular
district than provide sector specific services to different districts. A few district NGOs such
as SOLVE operate in more than one district and display characteristics similar to the central
NGOs but are less able to appropriate qualified personnel on demand.
7     Local NGOs.  There is great diversity in terms of experience and technical
competence within this category of NGOs. Some tend to be very small and have the
characteristics of CBOs (see para 9) while others provide services to one or more VDCs.
There are more than 600 local NGOs registered under the SWC. In the pilot project CBO
type local NGOs include Sunkosi Yuva Club (SYC) and Samaj Kalyan Kendra (SKK);
while those providing services other than to their immediate community include Paropkar
Prathmik Upachar Kendra (PPUK), and Dhanusa Sewa Samiti (DSS). Local NGOs enjoy
good rapport with the community and are able to render micro-assistance. Smaller local
NGOs do not have good book keeping practices which are required for larger projects
although they keep records of financial resources mobilized internally such as membership
fees, and proceeds from charity and cultural activities. One weakness of the majority of
local NGOs is that most of their staff are volunteers. They have few technical resources or



- 145 -
Annex 14
Page 3 of 6
expertise within their membership. Only a few are able to recruit technicians on contract or
tap technical assistance from the district line agencies.
8.    International NGOs. About 6 INGOs have been identified as potential SOs.
Helvetas is the largest INGO effort in the sector. Since 1976, it has implemented a total of
about 315 drinking water schemes mostly in the western region. It is also involved in water
quality monitoring and training in various aspects of water supply development such as
technical training, basic Water Supply & Sanitation training, health education, etc. Save the
Children Federation/USA was the first INGO to use non-formal education as an entry point
in its community development activities. Red Bama supports water supply, health
education, irrigation, forestry, agriculture, non-formal education, and income generating
activities. Lutheran World Service (LWS) have been functioning as an SA in different parts
of the country supporting other local organizations. More recently it has worked directly
with informal community groups with the intention that these groups would become
registered as local NGOs.
Community-Based Organizations/Informal Groups
9.    CBO/informal groups are generally informal organizations and their members are
themselves beneficiaries of their activities. Three types of CBOs are identified based on
origin. They include indigenous, self help and extemally formed CBOs. Indigenous CBOs
are informal groups that have evolved over one hundred years without outside assistance to
organize social life, pool labor, resources and express common interests and concems.
Indigenous cooperative groups are specific to a particular ethnic group, caste or community.
Self help organizations serve different functions, such as social groups, labor, and credit
associations. Informal credit association such as Dhikur exist among the Tibeto-Burman
speaking groups. Self help user groups have been formed to carry out specific development
activities and manage resources within communities. Membership in self-help groups is
based on residence and utilization of resources. Examples of strong self help groups are the
forestry user and several drinking water user groups.
10.     Externally  formed  CBOs evolved  during the implementation  of the
Decentralization Act of 1982 which made user groups as the focal point for development
activities. User group formation was imposed as a national policy managed by an executive
committee. As a result, majority of the user groups were formed without the users being
aware of its objectives and functions. Many of the beneficiaries perceive the formation as a
mere requirement to obtain external funds. The largest number of externally formed user
groups were reported to to be in the drinking water sub-sector.
11.   Indigenous and self-help CBOs are centers of community interaction and the focal
point of decision making. They are able to mobilize financial and human resources, and are
willing to undertake responsibilities to the project. For activities limited to the beneficiary
community the informal accounting system is perceived as appropriate and sufficient to
ensure financial transparency. There is a growing trend among CBOs within a locality to
form an association with a more formal structure to improve services to their members.



- 146 -
Annex 14
Page 4 of 6
CBO associations show considerable potential to function as SOs as they would be able to
utilize resources more efficiently than individual CBOs. Individual CBOs apppear to have
the capacity to implement one drinking water scheme per year.
Private Sector Firms
12.   Private Sector firms are for profit entities that may comprise the following: i) private
partnerships which are traditional consulting firms formed by several individuals offering
specialized knowledge and skills; ii) private limited companies. These tend to be traditional
consulting firms but usually include most construction companies which supply and
construct infrastructure building and other hardware; iii) engineering consultants which
include individuals and firms focussing on technical areas such as civil engineering,
technology transfer, construction and operation and maintenance; and iv) management and
training consultants providing professional management services to other organizations
including CBOs. All the above have traditionally offered a package of services ranging
from management, training and financial advice to planning and engineering feasibility
studies, to detailed design and specifications. A number of private firms could serve as SO
in the project as demonstrated under the JAKPAS pilot project.
13.   Selection of Support Organizations. To date, a total of 29 SOs have participated
under the Jakpas pilot project. They represent centrally based, district, and local NGOs,
CBOs, private firms and chapters of national NGOs, and an INGO. Support Organizations
have shown an inclination to work in areas where they have previously operated. Often
these are communities where the heads of NGO leaders or Board members have their roots.
As a result they enjoy considerable influence in these communities and have a "head start"
in terms of mobilizing the community.
14.   In communities where SOs are already operating they usually have requests for
water supplies on file. In others, SOs have identified communities where it felt there was a
need for improved supplies.  In communities served by the pilot project, SOs range from
marginalized groups (where there exists few basic resources and hard to reach communities)
to those easier to assist and located in accessible areas. Local NGOs are observed to be
based within a short distance of their target communities. District and centrally based NGOs
are stationed either at district headquarters or in Kathmandu.
15.    The assessment of SOs as partners for the Project indicates there would be at least
25 qualified SOs who would undertake between 100-125 schemes in the Project's first
Batch of schemes. Criteria for selecting SOs would identify organizations that have the
potential to undertake effective community based Rural Water Supply and Sanitation
schemes. All potential SOs would fill a prequalification form which would have
information on but not limited to sector(s) in which the organization operates and
specialization of activities, district(s) in which it operates, scale of operations, staffing in
terms of managerial and technical skills and registration status. Criteria would ensure that
SOs selected have the technical capacity (community development and construction) to
undertake community based Rural Water Supply and Sanitation schemes.



- 147 -
Annex 14
Page 5 of 6
B. Service Agencies
16.   Service Agencies (SAs) comprise institutions and private groups that would provide
services to the Board in areas such as communications material production, material supply,
training (software and hardware), technical site appraisal, monitoring and evaluation and
conducting special studies and impact assessments. The Board supports a core training
program in 5 areas to enable SOs to more effectively perform their function as support
organizations. The role of SAs would be to provide training services in areas such as
approaches to community planning and monitoring, hygiene and sanitation education,
survey, design and cost estimates and financial management, and assisting the Board in
conducting site appraisal and monitoring of schemes undertaken by beneficiary
communities with support from Support Organizations.
17.   Service Agencies are broadly categorized into 4 areas: training, monitoring and
evaluation, material development and suppliers and manufacturers. An assessment of
Service Agencies indicate most private training institutions are able to organize specialized
courses for particular clients as they have demonstrated capacity to recruit appropriate
personnel for particular tasks. The Jakpas pilot project has to date solicited the services of
about 15 SAs to provide inputs to SOs in hardware (eg., site appraisal), financial
management training and direct support to the pilot project management on auditing,
management information system, legal, and general management.
SAs for Traiing
18.   Training is offered through government line agencies, bilateral and multilateral
donors, national and international NGOs and private training institutions. Some training
organizations conduct training only on hardware such as plumbing, ferrocement tank
construction and O&M, while others on NFE, HSE, leadership building, and financial and
management training. The capacity of government training institutions to offer services to
the project is limited, both by the regular workload of their staff and bureaucratic
procedures involved in providing incentives for government staff. Staff assessment of a
sample of organizations engaged in training indicate that INGOs and private training
institutions are better able to expand their training capacity to meet demand.
Material Development
19.   There is only a handful of organizations that are capable of producing participatory
materials to support Rural Water Supply and Sanitation related activities. A few develop
health and NFE materials for neo-literates while some train government and
nongovernmental organizations on primary health care. The Health Education Section of
the MOH is responsible for producing health education materials. The section has not been
able to recruit personnel with creative abilities to produce innovative work. Organizations
with qualified staff although few in number are available. They would be able to develop



- 148 -
Annex 14
Page 6 of 6
materials for Rural Water Supply and Sanitation related activities with guidance on content
and material presentation.
Monitoring and Evaluation
20.   The organizations involved in these services include private consulting firms, and
national and international nongovernmental organizations. There are many qualified private
firms that would be able to appraise schemes for the Board, conduct formal evaluation of
projects and/or special studies. Many would also be able to provide technical personnel to
assist SOs to design and construct water supply schemes. Government line agencies are
primarily involved in conducting monitoring and evaluation (M&E) of their own programs
and generally do not render services to others.
Suppliers of Construction Materials
21.   Private manufacturing firms are able to complement government service delivery
systems. Most manufacturers conform to ISI standards but there is wide variety in the type
and quality of handpumps and fittings available in the market. Pipes are available on the
market as evidenced by the pilot project experience which demonstrates that materials are
being delivered within 2 weeks of placing order. The analysis of SAs providing
construction components indicates there is sufficient capacity in Nepal to meet the
requirements of the Project for pipes and handpumps. The one area in which Nepali
producers are weak is in fittings, the bulk of which continue to be imported from India.
C. Support Organization-Service Agency
22.   Support Organization Service Agency (SOSA) are organizations that combine
aspects of Support Organization (SO) and Service Agency (SA) working jointly in
partnership with communities and at the same time offering technical services to other
agencies. A number of them have begun to share their expertise by offering training and
technical services to other SOs, bilateral, multilateral, other INGOs and the Government.
As a group, SOSAs represent a fairly strong set of institutions. SOSAs involved in Rural
Water Supply and Sanitation typically have specialized staff in community development,
HSE and engineering and are able to provide training to their field staff and to other
organizations as well.
23.   Of the potential SOSAs reviewed, HELVETAS which has a more intensive
program to enhance women's participation spend a longer time in assessing needs and
organizing the community. Compared to the SOs reviewed SOSAs give more emphasis to
the software component and HSE in particular, is more integrated with water supplies. In
the JAKPAS pilot project, organizations that fit into this category are the centrally based
NGOs, INGOs, and private firms (eg., HELVETAS, DEVTEC). Two examples of NGOs
working with local NGO partners at the same time offers services to other NGOs and
INGOs are the South Asia Partnership Nepal (SAP-Nepal), and the Rural Development
Center of the United Mission to Nepal.



- 149 -
Annex 15
Page I of 9
NELPA
RUJRAL, WATER SUPPLY AND SANITATION PROJIFCT
Technical Criteria
Intoduction
1.    This technical annex summnarizes the design parameters recommended for use in the
Nepal Rural Water Supply and Sanitation Fund Development Board-Implementation
(Technical) Manuals listed in Attachment 1. All design criteria shall meet the environment
criteria in Annex 12.
2.    The project would incorporate an interactive planning process involving
communities and support organizations and would support the construction/rehabilitation of
900 gravity and tubewellldugwell schemes to serve an estimated design population of
550,000 in the hills and mountains and the terai.
3.    The project would provide appropriate sanitation facilities to approximately 40% of
project households in the hills and 50% in the terai through grants for a community
managed small revolving fund to provide loans for latrine construction, and a small
Institutional latrine component for selected schools and institutions such as health centres to
maximize demonstration effects.
Water Supply
4.    Types of Schemes: The Project would support the construction/rehabilitation
(making use, where feasible, structures/pipelines of existing systems that meet or are
rehabilitated to meet the technical standards) of schemes that meet the eligibility criteria as
set out in the Board Rules. The broad type of schemes in the mountains/hills comprise: (i)
gravity systems (open/closed) with spring/stream sources designed and operated for
continuous supply; and (ii) spring/kuwa protection. Schemes in the terai are: (i) tubewells
(possibly including deep tubewells under exceptional circumstances); and (ii) dugwells.
Catchment protection is also provided under the project.
5.    The Project would support schemes (built to a high standard of works in adherence
to the specification set out in the technical manuals) that meet the service level defined
below in section 10.8. If the community opts for service levels higher than this, the Project
would still support the scheme provided that the community bears the full additional cost
resulting from the higher service level and ensure satisfactory measures to prevent wastage
of water for example from private connections. Quality control aspects would also be
looked into under monitoring and evaluation.



- 150 -
Annex 15
Page 2 of 9
6.    The Project would fund, as the least cost means of providing safe water to the
community, the protection of existing polluted springs/kuwas if the proposed scheme does
not meet the need and economic eligibility criteria (i.e. no or little time savings). If a
community requests for a piped water supply instead of the identified spring/kuwa
protection works, the Project would provide grant funding for the scheme at levels
estimated for the least cost solution provided the community agrees to pay the additional
cost (over and above the spring protection) required for it to have the pipe water supply
system.
7.    Safe Yield. Source yield measurements are critical for sustainable water supply
schemes. Ideally sources for gravity systems should be measured over a period of a time in
the driest months. Sources would be measured at least once at the end of the dry season
during May and June and preferably twice (the two measurements being made in different
years) prior to construction. If the cumulative yield of sources is less than 0.1 I/s, such
sources shall not be selected.
8.    Pipeline,
8.1   Pipe Materials generally used in rural water supply schemes in Nepal are
locally manufactured high density polyethylene (HDPE) pipes which come in
pressure ratings of 2.0, 2.5, 4.0, 6.0 and 1 0.0 kg/sq.cm. The pipes normally used are
4.0, 6.0 and 10.0 kg/sq.cm.
8.2  In rocky terrain, gully or river crossing and connections into structures,
galvanized mild steel (GI) pipes are used.
8.3   Pipelines are laid with a well compacted earth cover of at least 90 cm. In
rocky terrain where laying at such a depth is not possible, metallic pipes (like GI
pipes) shall be used or HDPE pipes shall be properly protected to avoid damage.
9.    Optimization. Alternate design options would be considered to obtain the optimum
scheme acceptable to the community. The design criteria proposed takes this into
consideration in designing the various components of the schemes.  During designs of
schemes due attention shall be accorded to the operation and maintenance of the schemes.

Annex 15
Page 3 of 9

Page 5 of 9
(b)    shall be located and so designed that during low flows the design
discharge is available and during high flows when the stream is
turbid as much seminent as possible is excluded from entering the
transmission mains.
11.2 Transmission Main
Sizing: (a)    designed for rural water supplies in Nepal for average day demand;
(b)   for headloss calculations, actual length of the pipeline shall be
increased by 10%.
Routing:
(a)    shall avoid acute deflections in the alignment; where acute bends are
unavoidable anchor/thrust blocks shall be provided;
(b)   the alignment shall be as short as possible with no/minimum
river/stream/gully crossings and with gentle slopes to allow gravity
flow; when river/stream/gully crossings or (unavoidable) not so
stable routes are encountered, adequate protection against damage
shall be ensured;
(c)    air release valves shall be installed at appropriate high points where
air locks are likely to occur;
(d)    washout valye (gate valves) shall be installed at U-profiles to
facilitate draining of mains for repairs/cleaning;
(e)    break pressure tanks shall be provided, if required, when the
difference of pressure is 100 m in transmission mains and 60 m in
distribution mains when using HDPE pipes;
(f)    provision of taps/connections from the transmission mains to
communities en-route shall be avoided.
11.3    rvoirs:
(a)   shall be of ferrocement tanks and/or masonry (stone or brick) tanks
in cement sand mortar;
(b)   the option of central storage vs. storage in a number of parallel
tanks shall be considered.



- 154 -
Annex 15
Page 6 of 9
11.4   Distribution
(a)    shall be designed for peak demand;
(b)    shall be designed as branch system;
(c)    shall be provided with break pressure tanks, where required;
(d)   shall be provided with air valves/washout valves, if required.
11.5   Tapstands
(a)    shall be constructed of masonry in cement sand mortar;
(b)    shall have globe valves or ferrules installed to regulate flow through the
standposts.
12.   Tubewells and dug wells
12.1  Tubewells
(a)    shall be located at least 3Gm from a source of pollution such as a
latrine;
(b)    casing shall be sealed properly (by bentonite) against seepage of
polluted water through the annular space between casing and soil;
(c)    all available parts of the tubewell shall be disinfected before installation
to prevent contamination by & breeding of iron bacteria.
Shallow tubewells
(a)    shall normally be provided with GI top pipe, standard PVC screen and
locally available No. 6 suction type handpump or equivalent.
Deep tubewells
(a)    shall be fitted with steel or standard PVC lined casing and screen and
yield tested before installation of a locally available deepwell handpump
such as the India Mk 1m1 or equivalent. Where the yield is inadequate,
further development of the well shall be undertaken.



- 155 -
Annex 15
Page 7 of 9
12.2 Dugwells
(a)    shall be excavated below the dry season water table, lined with
concrete rings and fitted with a cover slab (with access opening) and an
appropriate handpump;
(b)    all wells shall be sealed to prevent ingress of surface water, shall be
constructed with a platform and drain, in the dry season,
(c)    platforms shall be constructed to suit the users with sufficient area for
washing and with drainage to a soakaway or a small garden or a field
etc.
13.   Rehabilitation. The possibility of rehabilitating existing sources and schemes and
incorporating them into new schemes shall be considered in both the hills and the terai if there
is a demand from the community. This could also include augmenting the supply to provide
acceptable service levels through existing systems.
14    Water quality. An environmental assessment of each of the potential sources shall be
carried out at the prefeasibility stage to identify those sources most at risk from pollution and
ensure that adequate measures, such as catchment protection and improved intake design, are
taken during and after construction. All tubewells and dugwells shall be disinfected after
construction and before commissioning. A random selection of completed schemes (initially
15%) would be tested twice - once each in the wet and dry seasons, for a limited number of
bacteriological, chemical and physical parameters to identify any trends in pollution of sources
as well as instances of pollution which require remedial action.
Catchment Protection
15. The Project would support catchment protection like plantation of trees to provide
safeguards against erosion/surface water runoff due to tree felling and pollution of sources.
Sanitation
16    Some design parameters for various types of institutional/household latrines that shall
be built through a community managed small revolving loan fund are as follows.
16. 1   Indicative technical factors for consideration in selecting the type of latrine:
(a)   type and bearing capacity of soil;
(b)    soil porosity, rate of infiltration,
(c)    depth of excavation possible manually;
(d)    risk of ground water pollution.



- 156 -
Annex 15
Page 8 of 9
16.2   Features to ensure quality control:
(a)    pits shall be lined with stone, brick or concrete rings   where there is a
danger of collapse;
(b)    slabs shall be directly over the pit or offset, and raised above ground
level on a stone or brick base to prevent surface water entering the pit;
slabs for simple pit or VIP latrines shall be constructed from stone with
mud bedding and pointing supported by timber or bamboo spanning
the pit, from planks cut from hard and insect resistant timber, or from
ferrocement or reinforced concrete;
(c)    slabs for pour-flush latrines shall be ferrocement or reinforced concrete
with a concrete or glass reinforced plastic pan built into the slab,
(d)    traps for pour-flush latrines shall be of standard design suitable for
flushing with one to two liters of water; superstructures shall be built
from local materials such as stone or bamboo matting for walls and
thatched roofs, and shall have a door to provide privacy;
(e)    the latrines shall be well ventilated.
17.    Drainage: Proper disposal of the waste water shall be ensured. The construction of
drainage works from the tap/standposts shall be the responsibility of the community.
